<a name="speech22"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.113">O, swear not by the moon, the inconstant moon,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.114">That monthly changes in her circled orb,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.115">Lest that thy love prove likewise variable.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech23"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.116">What shall I swear by?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech24"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.117">Do not swear at all;</a><br>
<a name="2.2.118">Or, if thou wilt, swear by thy gracious self,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.119">Which is the god of my idolatry,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.120">And I'll believe thee.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech25"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.121">If my heart's dear love--</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech26"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.122">Well, do not swear: although I joy in thee,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.123">I have no joy of this contract to-night:</a><br>
<a name="2.2.124">It is too rash, too unadvised, too sudden;</a><br>
<a name="2.2.125">Too like the lightning, which doth cease to be</a><br>
<a name="2.2.126">Ere one can say 'It lightens.' Sweet, good night!</a><br>
<a name="2.2.127">This bud of love, by summer's ripening breath,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.128">May prove a beauteous flower when next we meet.</a><br>
<a name="2.2.129">Good night, good night! as sweet repose and rest</a><br>
<a name="2.2.130">Come to thy heart as that within my breast!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech27"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.131">O, wilt thou leave me so unsatisfied?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech28"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.132">What satisfaction canst thou have to-night?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech29"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.133">The exchange of thy love's faithful vow for mine.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech30"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.134">I gave thee mine before thou didst request it:</a><br>
<a name="2.2.135">And yet I would it were to give again.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech31"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.136">Wouldst thou withdraw it? for what purpose, love?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech32"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.137">But to be frank, and give it thee again.</a><br>
<a name="2.2.138">And yet I wish but for the thing I have:</a><br>
<a name="2.2.139">My bounty is as boundless as the sea,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.140">My love as deep; the more I give to thee,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.141">The more I have, for both are infinite.</a><br>
<p><i>Nurse calls within</i></p>
<a name="2.2.142">I hear some noise within; dear love, adieu!</a><br>
<a name="2.2.143">Anon, good nurse! Sweet Montague, be true.</a><br>
<a name="2.2.144">Stay but a little, I will come again.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit, above</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech33"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.145">O blessed, blessed night! I am afeard.</a><br>
<a name="2.2.146">Being in night, all this is but a dream,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.147">Too flattering-sweet to be substantial.</a><br>
<p><i>Re-enter JULIET, above</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech34"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.148">Three words, dear Romeo, and good night indeed.</a><br>
<a name="2.2.149">If that thy bent of love be honourable,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.150">Thy purpose marriage, send me word to-morrow,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.151">By one that I'll procure to come to thee,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.152">Where and what time thou wilt perform the rite;</a><br>
<a name="2.2.153">And all my fortunes at thy foot I'll lay</a><br>
<a name="2.2.154">And follow thee my lord throughout the world.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech35"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.155">[Within]  Madam!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech36"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.156">I come, anon.--But if thou mean'st not well,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.157">I do beseech thee--</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech37"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.158">[Within]  Madam!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech38"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.159">By and by, I come:--</a><br>
<a name="2.2.160">To cease thy suit, and leave me to my grief:</a><br>
<a name="2.2.161">To-morrow will I send.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech39"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.162">So thrive my soul--</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech40"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.163">A thousand times good night!</a><br>
<p><i>Exit, above</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech41"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.164">A thousand times the worse, to want thy light.</a><br>
<a name="2.2.165">Love goes toward love, as schoolboys from</a><br>
<a name="2.2.166">their books,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.167">But love from love, toward school with heavy looks.</a><br>
<p><i>Retiring</i></p>
<p><i>Re-enter JULIET, above</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech42"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.168">Hist! Romeo, hist! O, for a falconer's voice,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.169">To lure this tassel-gentle back again!</a><br>
<a name="2.2.170">Bondage is hoarse, and may not speak aloud;</a><br>
<a name="2.2.171">Else would I tear the cave where Echo lies,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.172">And make her airy tongue more hoarse than mine,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.173">With repetition of my Romeo's name.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech43"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.174">It is my soul that calls upon my name:</a><br>
<a name="2.2.175">How silver-sweet sound lovers' tongues by night,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.176">Like softest music to attending ears!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech44"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.177">Romeo!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech45"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.178">     My dear?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech46"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.179">                  At what o'clock to-morrow</a><br>
<a name="2.2.180">Shall I send to thee?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech47"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.181">At the hour of nine.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech48"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.182">I will not fail: 'tis twenty years till then.</a><br>
<a name="2.2.183">I have forgot why I did call thee back.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech49"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.184">Let me stand here till thou remember it.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech50"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.185">I shall forget, to have thee still stand there,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.186">Remembering how I love thy company.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech51"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.187">And I'll still stay, to have thee still forget,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.188">Forgetting any other home but this.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech52"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.189">'Tis almost morning; I would have thee gone:</a><br>
<a name="2.2.190">And yet no further than a wanton's bird;</a><br>
<a name="2.2.191">Who lets it hop a little from her hand,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.192">Like a poor prisoner in his twisted gyves,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.193">And with a silk thread plucks it back again,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.194">So loving-jealous of his liberty.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech53"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.195">I would I were thy bird.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech54"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.196">Sweet, so would I:</a><br>
<a name="2.2.197">Yet I should kill thee with much cherishing.</a><br>
<a name="2.2.198">Good night, good night! parting is such</a><br>
<a name="2.2.199">sweet sorrow,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.200">That I shall say good night till it be morrow.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit above</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech55"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.2.201">Sleep dwell upon thine eyes, peace in thy breast!</a><br>
<a name="2.2.202">Would I were sleep and peace, so sweet to rest!</a><br>
<a name="2.2.203">Hence will I to my ghostly father's cell,</a><br>
<a name="2.2.204">His help to crave, and my dear hap to tell.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE III. Friar Laurence's cell.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter FRIAR LAURENCE, with a basket</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.1">The grey-eyed morn smiles on the frowning night,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.2">Chequering the eastern clouds with streaks of light,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.3">And flecked darkness like a drunkard reels</a><br>
<a name="2.3.4">From forth day's path and Titan's fiery wheels:</a><br>
<a name="2.3.5">Now, ere the sun advance his burning eye,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.6">The day to cheer and night's dank dew to dry,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.7">I must up-fill this osier cage of ours</a><br>
<a name="2.3.8">With baleful weeds and precious-juiced flowers.</a><br>
<a name="2.3.9">The earth that's nature's mother is her tomb;</a><br>
<a name="2.3.10">What is her burying grave that is her womb,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.11">And from her womb children of divers kind</a><br>
<a name="2.3.12">We sucking on her natural bosom find,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.13">Many for many virtues excellent,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.14">None but for some and yet all different.</a><br>
<a name="2.3.15">O, mickle is the powerful grace that lies</a><br>
<a name="2.3.16">In herbs, plants, stones, and their true qualities:</a><br>
<a name="2.3.17">For nought so vile that on the earth doth live</a><br>
<a name="2.3.18">But to the earth some special good doth give,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.19">Nor aught so good but strain'd from that fair use</a><br>
<a name="2.3.20">Revolts from true birth, stumbling on abuse:</a><br>
<a name="2.3.21">Virtue itself turns vice, being misapplied;</a><br>
<a name="2.3.22">And vice sometimes by action dignified.</a><br>
<a name="2.3.23">Within the infant rind of this small flower</a><br>
<a name="2.3.24">Poison hath residence and medicine power:</a><br>
<a name="2.3.25">For this, being smelt, with that part cheers each part;</a><br>
<a name="2.3.26">Being tasted, slays all senses with the heart.</a><br>
<a name="2.3.27">Two such opposed kings encamp them still</a><br>
<a name="2.3.28">In man as well as herbs, grace and rude will;</a><br>
<a name="2.3.29">And where the worser is predominant,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.30">Full soon the canker death eats up that plant.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter ROMEO</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.31">Good morrow, father.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.32">Benedicite!</a><br>
<a name="2.3.33">What early tongue so sweet saluteth me?</a><br>
<a name="2.3.34">Young son, it argues a distemper'd head</a><br>
<a name="2.3.35">So soon to bid good morrow to thy bed:</a><br>
<a name="2.3.36">Care keeps his watch in every old man's eye,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.37">And where care lodges, sleep will never lie;</a><br>
<a name="2.3.38">But where unbruised youth with unstuff'd brain</a><br>
<a name="2.3.39">Doth couch his limbs, there golden sleep doth reign:</a><br>
<a name="2.3.40">Therefore thy earliness doth me assure</a><br>
<a name="2.3.41">Thou art up-roused by some distemperature;</a><br>
<a name="2.3.42">Or if not so, then here I hit it right,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.43">Our Romeo hath not been in bed to-night.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.44">That last is true; the sweeter rest was mine.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.45">God pardon sin! wast thou with Rosaline?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.46">With Rosaline, my ghostly father? no;</a><br>
<a name="2.3.47">I have forgot that name, and that name's woe.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.48">That's my good son: but where hast thou been, then?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.49">I'll tell thee, ere thou ask it me again.</a><br>
<a name="2.3.50">I have been feasting with mine enemy,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.51">Where on a sudden one hath wounded me,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.52">That's by me wounded: both our remedies</a><br>
<a name="2.3.53">Within thy help and holy physic lies:</a><br>
<a name="2.3.54">I bear no hatred, blessed man, for, lo,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.55">My intercession likewise steads my foe.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.56">Be plain, good son, and homely in thy drift;</a><br>
<a name="2.3.57">Riddling confession finds but riddling shrift.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.58">Then plainly know my heart's dear love is set</a><br>
<a name="2.3.59">On the fair daughter of rich Capulet:</a><br>
<a name="2.3.60">As mine on hers, so hers is set on mine;</a><br>
<a name="2.3.61">And all combined, save what thou must combine</a><br>
<a name="2.3.62">By holy marriage: when and where and how</a><br>
<a name="2.3.63">We met, we woo'd and made exchange of vow,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.64">I'll tell thee as we pass; but this I pray,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.65">That thou consent to marry us to-day.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.66">Holy Saint Francis, what a change is here!</a><br>
<a name="2.3.67">Is Rosaline, whom thou didst love so dear,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.68">So soon forsaken? young men's love then lies</a><br>
<a name="2.3.69">Not truly in their hearts, but in their eyes.</a><br>
<a name="2.3.70">Jesu Maria, what a deal of brine</a><br>
<a name="2.3.71">Hath wash'd thy sallow cheeks for Rosaline!</a><br>
<a name="2.3.72">How much salt water thrown away in waste,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.73">To season love, that of it doth not taste!</a><br>
<a name="2.3.74">The sun not yet thy sighs from heaven clears,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.75">Thy old groans ring yet in my ancient ears;</a><br>
<a name="2.3.76">Lo, here upon thy cheek the stain doth sit</a><br>
<a name="2.3.77">Of an old tear that is not wash'd off yet:</a><br>
<a name="2.3.78">If e'er thou wast thyself and these woes thine,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.79">Thou and these woes were all for Rosaline:</a><br>
<a name="2.3.80">And art thou changed? pronounce this sentence then,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.81">Women may fall, when there's no strength in men.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.82">Thou chid'st me oft for loving Rosaline.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.83">For doting, not for loving, pupil mine.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.84">And bad'st me bury love.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.85">Not in a grave,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.86">To lay one in, another out to have.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.87">I pray thee, chide not; she whom I love now</a><br>
<a name="2.3.88">Doth grace for grace and love for love allow;</a><br>
<a name="2.3.89">The other did not so.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.90">O, she knew well</a><br>
<a name="2.3.91">Thy love did read by rote and could not spell.</a><br>
<a name="2.3.92">But come, young waverer, come, go with me,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.93">In one respect I'll thy assistant be;</a><br>
<a name="2.3.94">For this alliance may so happy prove,</a><br>
<a name="2.3.95">To turn your households' rancour to pure love.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.96">O, let us hence; I stand on sudden haste.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech19"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.3.97">Wisely and slow; they stumble that run fast.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE IV. A street.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter BENVOLIO and MERCUTIO</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.1">Where the devil should this Romeo be?</a><br>
<a name="2.4.2">Came he not home to-night?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.3">Not to his father's; I spoke with his man.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.4">Ah, that same pale hard-hearted wench, that Rosaline.</a><br>
<a name="2.4.5">Torments him so, that he will sure run mad.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.6">Tybalt, the kinsman of old Capulet,</a><br>
<a name="2.4.7">Hath sent a letter to his father's house.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.8">A challenge, on my life.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.9">Romeo will answer it.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.10">Any man that can write may answer a letter.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.11">Nay, he will answer the letter's master, how he</a><br>
<a name="2.4.12">dares, being dared.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.13">Alas poor Romeo! he is already dead; stabbed with a</a><br>
<a name="2.4.14">white wench's black eye; shot through the ear with a</a><br>
<a name="2.4.15">love-song; the very pin of his heart cleft with the</a><br>
<a name="2.4.16">blind bow-boy's butt-shaft: and is he a man to</a><br>
<a name="2.4.17">encounter Tybalt?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.18">Why, what is Tybalt?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.19">More than prince of cats, I can tell you. O, he is</a><br>
<a name="2.4.20">the courageous captain of compliments. He fights as</a><br>
<a name="2.4.21">you sing prick-song, keeps time, distance, and</a><br>
<a name="2.4.22">proportion; rests me his minim rest, one, two, and</a><br>
<a name="2.4.23">the third in your bosom: the very butcher of a silk</a><br>
<a name="2.4.24">button, a duellist, a duellist; a gentleman of the</a><br>
<a name="2.4.25">very first house, of the first and second cause:</a><br>
<a name="2.4.26">ah, the immortal passado! the punto reverso! the</a><br>
<a name="2.4.27">hai!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.28">The what?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.29">The pox of such antic, lisping, affecting</a><br>
<a name="2.4.30">fantasticoes; these new tuners of accents! 'By Jesu,</a><br>
<a name="2.4.31">a very good blade! a very tall man! a very good</a><br>
<a name="2.4.32">whore!' Why, is not this a lamentable thing,</a><br>
<a name="2.4.33">grandsire, that we should be thus afflicted with</a><br>
<a name="2.4.34">these strange flies, these fashion-mongers, these</a><br>
<a name="2.4.35">perdona-mi's, who stand so much on the new form,</a><br>
<a name="2.4.36">that they cannot at ease on the old bench? O, their</a><br>
<a name="2.4.37">bones, their bones!</a><br>
<p><i>Enter ROMEO</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.38">Here comes Romeo, here comes Romeo.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.39">Without his roe, like a dried herring: flesh, flesh,</a><br>
<a name="2.4.40">how art thou fishified! Now is he for the numbers</a><br>
<a name="2.4.41">that Petrarch flowed in: Laura to his lady was but a</a><br>
<a name="2.4.42">kitchen-wench; marry, she had a better love to</a><br>
<a name="2.4.43">be-rhyme her; Dido a dowdy; Cleopatra a gipsy;</a><br>
<a name="2.4.44">Helen and Hero hildings and harlots; Thisbe a grey</a><br>
<a name="2.4.45">eye or so, but not to the purpose. Signior</a><br>
<a name="2.4.46">Romeo, bon jour! there's a French salutation</a><br>
<a name="2.4.47">to your French slop. You gave us the counterfeit</a><br>
<a name="2.4.48">fairly last night.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.49">Good morrow to you both. What counterfeit did I give you?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.50">The ship, sir, the slip; can you not conceive?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.51">Pardon, good Mercutio, my business was great; and in</a><br>
<a name="2.4.52">such a case as mine a man may strain courtesy.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech19"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.53">That's as much as to say, such a case as yours</a><br>
<a name="2.4.54">constrains a man to bow in the hams.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech20"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.55">Meaning, to court'sy.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech21"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.56">Thou hast most kindly hit it.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech22"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.57">A most courteous exposition.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech23"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.58">Nay, I am the very pink of courtesy.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech24"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.59">Pink for flower.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech25"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.60">Right.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech26"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.61">Why, then is my pump well flowered.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech27"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.62">Well said: follow me this jest now till thou hast</a><br>
<a name="2.4.63">worn out thy pump, that when the single sole of it</a><br>
<a name="2.4.64">is worn, the jest may remain after the wearing sole singular.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech28"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.65">O single-soled jest, solely singular for the</a><br>
<a name="2.4.66">singleness.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech29"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.67">Come between us, good Benvolio; my wits faint.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech30"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.68">Switch and spurs, switch and spurs; or I'll cry a match.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech31"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.69">Nay, if thy wits run the wild-goose chase, I have</a><br>
<a name="2.4.70">done, for thou hast more of the wild-goose in one of</a><br>
<a name="2.4.71">thy wits than, I am sure, I have in my whole five:</a><br>
<a name="2.4.72">was I with you there for the goose?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech32"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.73">Thou wast never with me for any thing when thou wast</a><br>
<a name="2.4.74">not there for the goose.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech33"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.75">I will bite thee by the ear for that jest.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech34"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.76">Nay, good goose, bite not.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech35"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.77">Thy wit is a very bitter sweeting; it is a most</a><br>
<a name="2.4.78">sharp sauce.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech36"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.79">And is it not well served in to a sweet goose?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech37"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.80">O here's a wit of cheveril, that stretches from an</a><br>
<a name="2.4.81">inch narrow to an ell broad!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech38"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.82">I stretch it out for that word 'broad;' which added</a><br>
<a name="2.4.83">to the goose, proves thee far and wide a broad goose.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech39"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.84">Why, is not this better now than groaning for love?</a><br>
<a name="2.4.85">now art thou sociable, now art thou Romeo; now art</a><br>
<a name="2.4.86">thou what thou art, by art as well as by nature:</a><br>
<a name="2.4.87">for this drivelling love is like a great natural,</a><br>
<a name="2.4.88">that runs lolling up and down to hide his bauble in a hole.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech40"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.89">Stop there, stop there.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech41"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.90">Thou desirest me to stop in my tale against the hair.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech42"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.91">Thou wouldst else have made thy tale large.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech43"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.92">O, thou art deceived; I would have made it short:</a><br>
<a name="2.4.93">for I was come to the whole depth of my tale; and</a><br>
<a name="2.4.94">meant, indeed, to occupy the argument no longer.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech44"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.95">Here's goodly gear!</a><br>
<p><i>Enter Nurse and PETER</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech45"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.96">A sail, a sail!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech46"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.97">Two, two; a shirt and a smock.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech47"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.98">Peter!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech48"><b>PETER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.99">Anon!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech49"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.100">My fan, Peter.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech50"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.101">Good Peter, to hide her face; for her fan's the</a><br>
<a name="2.4.102">fairer face.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech51"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.103">God ye good morrow, gentlemen.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech52"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.104">God ye good den, fair gentlewoman.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech53"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.105">Is it good den?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech54"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.106">'Tis no less, I tell you, for the bawdy hand of the</a><br>
<a name="2.4.107">dial is now upon the prick of noon.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech55"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.108">Out upon you! what a man are you!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech56"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.109">One, gentlewoman, that God hath made for himself to</a><br>
<a name="2.4.110">mar.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech57"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.111">By my troth, it is well said; 'for himself to mar,'</a><br>
<a name="2.4.112">quoth a'? Gentlemen, can any of you tell me where I</a><br>
<a name="2.4.113">may find the young Romeo?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech58"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.114">I can tell you; but young Romeo will be older when</a><br>
<a name="2.4.115">you have found him than he was when you sought him:</a><br>
<a name="2.4.116">I am the youngest of that name, for fault of a worse.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech59"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.117">You say well.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech60"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.118">Yea, is the worst well? very well took, i' faith;</a><br>
<a name="2.4.119">wisely, wisely.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech61"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.120">if you be he, sir, I desire some confidence with</a><br>
<a name="2.4.121">you.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech62"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.122">She will indite him to some supper.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech63"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.123">A bawd, a bawd, a bawd! so ho!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech64"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.124">What hast thou found?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech65"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.125">No hare, sir; unless a hare, sir, in a lenten pie,</a><br>
<a name="2.4.126">that is something stale and hoar ere it be spent.</a><br>
<p><i>Sings</i></p>
<a name="2.4.127">An old hare hoar,</a><br>
<a name="2.4.128">And an old hare hoar,</a><br>
<a name="2.4.129">Is very good meat in lent</a><br>
<a name="2.4.130">But a hare that is hoar</a><br>
<a name="2.4.131">Is too much for a score,</a><br>
<a name="2.4.132">When it hoars ere it be spent.</a><br>
<a name="2.4.133">Romeo, will you come to your father's? we'll</a><br>
<a name="2.4.134">to dinner, thither.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech66"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.135">I will follow you.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech67"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.136">Farewell, ancient lady; farewell,</a><br>
<p><i>Singing</i></p>
<a name="2.4.137">'lady, lady, lady.'</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt MERCUTIO and BENVOLIO</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech68"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.138">Marry, farewell! I pray you, sir, what saucy</a><br>
<a name="2.4.139">merchant was this, that was so full of his ropery?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech69"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.140">A gentleman, nurse, that loves to hear himself talk,</a><br>
<a name="2.4.141">and will speak more in a minute than he will stand</a><br>
<a name="2.4.142">to in a month.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech70"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.143">An a' speak any thing against me, I'll take him</a><br>
<a name="2.4.144">down, an a' were lustier than he is, and twenty such</a><br>
<a name="2.4.145">Jacks; and if I cannot, I'll find those that shall.</a><br>
<a name="2.4.146">Scurvy knave! I am none of his flirt-gills; I am</a><br>
<a name="2.4.147">none of his skains-mates. And thou must stand by</a><br>
<a name="2.4.148">too, and suffer every knave to use me at his pleasure?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech71"><b>PETER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.149">I saw no man use you a pleasure; if I had, my weapon</a><br>
<a name="2.4.150">should quickly have been out, I warrant you: I dare</a><br>
<a name="2.4.151">draw as soon as another man, if I see occasion in a</a><br>
<a name="2.4.152">good quarrel, and the law on my side.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech72"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.153">Now, afore God, I am so vexed, that every part about</a><br>
<a name="2.4.154">me quivers. Scurvy knave! Pray you, sir, a word:</a><br>
<a name="2.4.155">and as I told you, my young lady bade me inquire you</a><br>
<a name="2.4.156">out; what she bade me say, I will keep to myself:</a><br>
<a name="2.4.157">but first let me tell ye, if ye should lead her into</a><br>
<a name="2.4.158">a fool's paradise, as they say, it were a very gross</a><br>
<a name="2.4.159">kind of behavior, as they say: for the gentlewoman</a><br>
<a name="2.4.160">is young; and, therefore, if you should deal double</a><br>
<a name="2.4.161">with her, truly it were an ill thing to be offered</a><br>
<a name="2.4.162">to any gentlewoman, and very weak dealing.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech73"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.163">Nurse, commend me to thy lady and mistress. I</a><br>
<a name="2.4.164">protest unto thee--</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech74"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.165">Good heart, and, i' faith, I will tell her as much:</a><br>
<a name="2.4.166">Lord, Lord, she will be a joyful woman.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech75"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.167">What wilt thou tell her, nurse? thou dost not mark me.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech76"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.168">I will tell her, sir, that you do protest; which, as</a><br>
<a name="2.4.169">I take it, is a gentlemanlike offer.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech77"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.170">Bid her devise</a><br>
<a name="2.4.171">Some means to come to shrift this afternoon;</a><br>
<a name="2.4.172">And there she shall at Friar Laurence' cell</a><br>
<a name="2.4.173">Be shrived and married. Here is for thy pains.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech78"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.174">No truly sir; not a penny.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech79"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.175">Go to; I say you shall.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech80"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.176">This afternoon, sir? well, she shall be there.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech81"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.177">And stay, good nurse, behind the abbey wall:</a><br>
<a name="2.4.178">Within this hour my man shall be with thee</a><br>
<a name="2.4.179">And bring thee cords made like a tackled stair;</a><br>
<a name="2.4.180">Which to the high top-gallant of my joy</a><br>
<a name="2.4.181">Must be my convoy in the secret night.</a><br>
<a name="2.4.182">Farewell; be trusty, and I'll quit thy pains:</a><br>
<a name="2.4.183">Farewell; commend me to thy mistress.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech82"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.184">Now God in heaven bless thee! Hark you, sir.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech83"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.185">What say'st thou, my dear nurse?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech84"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.186">Is your man secret? Did you ne'er hear say,</a><br>
<a name="2.4.187">Two may keep counsel, putting one away?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech85"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.188">I warrant thee, my man's as true as steel.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech86"><b>NURSE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.189">Well, sir; my mistress is the sweetest lady--Lord,</a><br>
<a name="2.4.190">Lord! when 'twas a little prating thing:--O, there</a><br>
<a name="2.4.191">is a nobleman in town, one Paris, that would fain</a><br>
<a name="2.4.192">lay knife aboard; but she, good soul, had as lief</a><br>
<a name="2.4.193">see a toad, a very toad, as see him. I anger her</a><br>
<a name="2.4.194">sometimes and tell her that Paris is the properer</a><br>
<a name="2.4.195">man; but, I'll warrant you, when I say so, she looks</a><br>
<a name="2.4.196">as pale as any clout in the versal world. Doth not</a><br>
<a name="2.4.197">rosemary and Romeo begin both with a letter?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech87"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.198">Ay, nurse; what of that? both with an R.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech88"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.199">Ah. mocker! that's the dog's name; R is for</a><br>
<a name="2.4.200">the--No; I know it begins with some other</a><br>
<a name="2.4.201">letter:--and she hath the prettiest sententious of</a><br>
<a name="2.4.202">it, of you and rosemary, that it would do you good</a><br>
<a name="2.4.203">to hear it.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech89"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.204">Commend me to thy lady.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech90"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.205">Ay, a thousand times.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit Romeo</i></p>
<a name="2.4.206">Peter!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech91"><b>PETER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.207">Anon!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech92"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.4.208">Peter, take my fan, and go before and apace.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE V. Capulet's orchard.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter JULIET</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.1">The clock struck nine when I did send the nurse;</a><br>
<a name="2.5.2">In half an hour she promised to return.</a><br>
<a name="2.5.3">Perchance she cannot meet him: that's not so.</a><br>
<a name="2.5.4">O, she is lame! love's heralds should be thoughts,</a><br>
<a name="2.5.5">Which ten times faster glide than the sun's beams,</a><br>
<a name="2.5.6">Driving back shadows over louring hills:</a><br>
<a name="2.5.7">Therefore do nimble-pinion'd doves draw love,</a><br>
<a name="2.5.8">And therefore hath the wind-swift Cupid wings.</a><br>
<a name="2.5.9">Now is the sun upon the highmost hill</a><br>
<a name="2.5.10">Of this day's journey, and from nine till twelve</a><br>
<a name="2.5.11">Is three long hours, yet she is not come.</a><br>
<a name="2.5.12">Had she affections and warm youthful blood,</a><br>
<a name="2.5.13">She would be as swift in motion as a ball;</a><br>
<a name="2.5.14">My words would bandy her to my sweet love,</a><br>
<a name="2.5.15">And his to me:</a><br>
<a name="2.5.16">But old folks, many feign as they were dead;</a><br>
<a name="2.5.17">Unwieldy, slow, heavy and pale as lead.</a><br>
<a name="2.5.18">O God, she comes!</a><br>
<p><i>Enter Nurse and PETER</i></p>
<a name="2.5.19">O honey nurse, what news?</a><br>
<a name="2.5.20">Hast thou met with him? Send thy man away.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.21">Peter, stay at the gate.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit PETER</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.22">Now, good sweet nurse,--O Lord, why look'st thou sad?</a><br>
<a name="2.5.23">Though news be sad, yet tell them merrily;</a><br>
<a name="2.5.24">If good, thou shamest the music of sweet news</a><br>
<a name="2.5.25">By playing it to me with so sour a face.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.26">I am a-weary, give me leave awhile:</a><br>
<a name="2.5.27">Fie, how my bones ache! what a jaunt have I had!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.28">I would thou hadst my bones, and I thy news:</a><br>
<a name="2.5.29">Nay, come, I pray thee, speak; good, good nurse, speak.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.30">Jesu, what haste? can you not stay awhile?</a><br>
<a name="2.5.31">Do you not see that I am out of breath?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.32">How art thou out of breath, when thou hast breath</a><br>
<a name="2.5.33">To say to me that thou art out of breath?</a><br>
<a name="2.5.34">The excuse that thou dost make in this delay</a><br>
<a name="2.5.35">Is longer than the tale thou dost excuse.</a><br>
<a name="2.5.36">Is thy news good, or bad? answer to that;</a><br>
<a name="2.5.37">Say either, and I'll stay the circumstance:</a><br>
<a name="2.5.38">Let me be satisfied, is't good or bad?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.39">Well, you have made a simple choice; you know not</a><br>
<a name="2.5.40">how to choose a man: Romeo! no, not he; though his</a><br>
<a name="2.5.41">face be better than any man's, yet his leg excels</a><br>
<a name="2.5.42">all men's; and for a hand, and a foot, and a body,</a><br>
<a name="2.5.43">though they be not to be talked on, yet they are</a><br>
<a name="2.5.44">past compare: he is not the flower of courtesy,</a><br>
<a name="2.5.45">but, I'll warrant him, as gentle as a lamb. Go thy</a><br>
<a name="2.5.46">ways, wench; serve God. What, have you dined at home?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.47">No, no: but all this did I know before.</a><br>
<a name="2.5.48">What says he of our marriage? what of that?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.49">Lord, how my head aches! what a head have I!</a><br>
<a name="2.5.50">It beats as it would fall in twenty pieces.</a><br>
<a name="2.5.51">My back o' t' other side,--O, my back, my back!</a><br>
<a name="2.5.52">Beshrew your heart for sending me about,</a><br>
<a name="2.5.53">To catch my death with jaunting up and down!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.54">I' faith, I am sorry that thou art not well.</a><br>
<a name="2.5.55">Sweet, sweet, sweet nurse, tell me, what says my love?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.56">Your love says, like an honest gentleman, and a</a><br>
<a name="2.5.57">courteous, and a kind, and a handsome, and, I</a><br>
<a name="2.5.58">warrant, a virtuous,--Where is your mother?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.59">Where is my mother! why, she is within;</a><br>
<a name="2.5.60">Where should she be? How oddly thou repliest!</a><br>
<a name="2.5.61">'Your love says, like an honest gentleman,</a><br>
<a name="2.5.62">Where is your mother?'</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.63">O God's lady dear!</a><br>
<a name="2.5.64">Are you so hot? marry, come up, I trow;</a><br>
<a name="2.5.65">Is this the poultice for my aching bones?</a><br>
<a name="2.5.66">Henceforward do your messages yourself.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.67">Here's such a coil! come, what says Romeo?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.68">Have you got leave to go to shrift to-day?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.69">I have.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.70">Then hie you hence to Friar Laurence' cell;</a><br>
<a name="2.5.71">There stays a husband to make you a wife:</a><br>
<a name="2.5.72">Now comes the wanton blood up in your cheeks,</a><br>
<a name="2.5.73">They'll be in scarlet straight at any news.</a><br>
<a name="2.5.74">Hie you to church; I must another way,</a><br>
<a name="2.5.75">To fetch a ladder, by the which your love</a><br>
<a name="2.5.76">Must climb a bird's nest soon when it is dark:</a><br>
<a name="2.5.77">I am the drudge and toil in your delight,</a><br>
<a name="2.5.78">But you shall bear the burden soon at night.</a><br>
<a name="2.5.79">Go; I'll to dinner: hie you to the cell.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech19"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.5.80">Hie to high fortune! Honest nurse, farewell.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE VI. Friar Laurence's cell.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter FRIAR LAURENCE and ROMEO</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.6.1">So smile the heavens upon this holy act,</a><br>
<a name="2.6.2">That after hours with sorrow chide us not!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.6.3">Amen, amen! but come what sorrow can,</a><br>
<a name="2.6.4">It cannot countervail the exchange of joy</a><br>
<a name="2.6.5">That one short minute gives me in her sight:</a><br>
<a name="2.6.6">Do thou but close our hands with holy words,</a><br>
<a name="2.6.7">Then love-devouring death do what he dare;</a><br>
<a name="2.6.8">It is enough I may but call her mine.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.6.9">These violent delights have violent ends</a><br>
<a name="2.6.10">And in their triumph die, like fire and powder,</a><br>
<a name="2.6.11">Which as they kiss consume: the sweetest honey</a><br>
<a name="2.6.12">Is loathsome in his own deliciousness</a><br>
<a name="2.6.13">And in the taste confounds the appetite:</a><br>
<a name="2.6.14">Therefore love moderately; long love doth so;</a><br>
<a name="2.6.15">Too swift arrives as tardy as too slow.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter JULIET</i></p>
<a name="2.6.16">Here comes the lady: O, so light a foot</a><br>
<a name="2.6.17">Will ne'er wear out the everlasting flint:</a><br>
<a name="2.6.18">A lover may bestride the gossamer</a><br>
<a name="2.6.19">That idles in the wanton summer air,</a><br>
<a name="2.6.20">And yet not fall; so light is vanity.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.6.21">Good even to my ghostly confessor.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.6.22">Romeo shall thank thee, daughter, for us both.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.6.23">As much to him, else is his thanks too much.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.6.24">Ah, Juliet, if the measure of thy joy</a><br>
<a name="2.6.25">Be heap'd like mine and that thy skill be more</a><br>
<a name="2.6.26">To blazon it, then sweeten with thy breath</a><br>
<a name="2.6.27">This neighbour air, and let rich music's tongue</a><br>
<a name="2.6.28">Unfold the imagined happiness that both</a><br>
<a name="2.6.29">Receive in either by this dear encounter.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.6.30">Conceit, more rich in matter than in words,</a><br>
<a name="2.6.31">Brags of his substance, not of ornament:</a><br>
<a name="2.6.32">They are but beggars that can count their worth;</a><br>
<a name="2.6.33">But my true love is grown to such excess</a><br>
<a name="2.6.34">I cannot sum up sum of half my wealth.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="2.6.35">Come, come with me, and we will make short work;</a><br>
<a name="2.6.36">For, by your leaves, you shall not stay alone</a><br>
<a name="2.6.37">Till holy church incorporate two in one.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote><p>
</p><h3>ACT III</h3>
<h3>SCENE I. A public place.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter MERCUTIO, BENVOLIO, Page, and Servants</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.1">I pray thee, good Mercutio, let's retire:</a><br>
<a name="3.1.2">The day is hot, the Capulets abroad,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.3">And, if we meet, we shall not scape a brawl;</a><br>
<a name="3.1.4">For now, these hot days, is the mad blood stirring.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.5">Thou art like one of those fellows that when he</a><br>
<a name="3.1.6">enters the confines of a tavern claps me his sword</a><br>
<a name="3.1.7">upon the table and says 'God send me no need of</a><br>
<a name="3.1.8">thee!' and by the operation of the second cup draws</a><br>
<a name="3.1.9">it on the drawer, when indeed there is no need.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.10">Am I like such a fellow?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.11">Come, come, thou art as hot a Jack in thy mood as</a><br>
<a name="3.1.12">any in Italy, and as soon moved to be moody, and as</a><br>
<a name="3.1.13">soon moody to be moved.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.14">And what to?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.15">Nay, an there were two such, we should have none</a><br>
<a name="3.1.16">shortly, for one would kill the other. Thou! why,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.17">thou wilt quarrel with a man that hath a hair more,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.18">or a hair less, in his beard, than thou hast: thou</a><br>
<a name="3.1.19">wilt quarrel with a man for cracking nuts, having no</a><br>
<a name="3.1.20">other reason but because thou hast hazel eyes: what</a><br>
<a name="3.1.21">eye but such an eye would spy out such a quarrel?</a><br>
<a name="3.1.22">Thy head is as fun of quarrels as an egg is full of</a><br>
<a name="3.1.23">meat, and yet thy head hath been beaten as addle as</a><br>
<a name="3.1.24">an egg for quarrelling: thou hast quarrelled with a</a><br>
<a name="3.1.25">man for coughing in the street, because he hath</a><br>
<a name="3.1.26">wakened thy dog that hath lain asleep in the sun:</a><br>
<a name="3.1.27">didst thou not fall out with a tailor for wearing</a><br>
<a name="3.1.28">his new doublet before Easter? with another, for</a><br>
<a name="3.1.29">tying his new shoes with old riband? and yet thou</a><br>
<a name="3.1.30">wilt tutor me from quarrelling!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.31">An I were so apt to quarrel as thou art, any man</a><br>
<a name="3.1.32">should buy the fee-simple of my life for an hour and a quarter.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.33">The fee-simple! O simple!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.34">By my head, here come the Capulets.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.35">By my heel, I care not.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter TYBALT and others</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>TYBALT</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.36">Follow me close, for I will speak to them.</a><br>
<a name="3.1.37">Gentlemen, good den: a word with one of you.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.38">And but one word with one of us? couple it with</a><br>
<a name="3.1.39">something; make it a word and a blow.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>TYBALT</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.40">You shall find me apt enough to that, sir, an you</a><br>
<a name="3.1.41">will give me occasion.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.42">Could you not take some occasion without giving?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>TYBALT</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.43">Mercutio, thou consort'st with Romeo,--</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.44">Consort! what, dost thou make us minstrels? an</a><br>
<a name="3.1.45">thou make minstrels of us, look to hear nothing but</a><br>
<a name="3.1.46">discords: here's my fiddlestick; here's that shall</a><br>
<a name="3.1.47">make you dance. 'Zounds, consort!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.48">We talk here in the public haunt of men:</a><br>
<a name="3.1.49">Either withdraw unto some private place,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.50">And reason coldly of your grievances,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.51">Or else depart; here all eyes gaze on us.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.52">Men's eyes were made to look, and let them gaze;</a><br>
<a name="3.1.53">I will not budge for no man's pleasure, I.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter ROMEO</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech19"><b>TYBALT</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.54">Well, peace be with you, sir: here comes my man.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech20"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.55">But I'll be hanged, sir, if he wear your livery:</a><br>
<a name="3.1.56">Marry, go before to field, he'll be your follower;</a><br>
<a name="3.1.57">Your worship in that sense may call him 'man.'</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech21"><b>TYBALT</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.58">Romeo, the hate I bear thee can afford</a><br>
<a name="3.1.59">No better term than this,--thou art a villain.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech22"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.60">Tybalt, the reason that I have to love thee</a><br>
<a name="3.1.61">Doth much excuse the appertaining rage</a><br>
<a name="3.1.62">To such a greeting: villain am I none;</a><br>
<a name="3.1.63">Therefore farewell; I see thou know'st me not.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech23"><b>TYBALT</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.64">Boy, this shall not excuse the injuries</a><br>
<a name="3.1.65">That thou hast done me; therefore turn and draw.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech24"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.66">I do protest, I never injured thee,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.67">But love thee better than thou canst devise,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.68">Till thou shalt know the reason of my love:</a><br>
<a name="3.1.69">And so, good Capulet,--which name I tender</a><br>
<a name="3.1.70">As dearly as my own,--be satisfied.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech25"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.71">O calm, dishonourable, vile submission!</a><br>
<a name="3.1.72">Alla stoccata carries it away.</a><br>
<p><i>Draws</i></p>
<a name="3.1.73">Tybalt, you rat-catcher, will you walk?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech26"><b>TYBALT</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.74">What wouldst thou have with me?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech27"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.75">Good king of cats, nothing but one of your nine</a><br>
<a name="3.1.76">lives; that I mean to make bold withal, and as you</a><br>
<a name="3.1.77">shall use me hereafter, drybeat the rest of the</a><br>
<a name="3.1.78">eight. Will you pluck your sword out of his pitcher</a><br>
<a name="3.1.79">by the ears? make haste, lest mine be about your</a><br>
<a name="3.1.80">ears ere it be out.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech28"><b>TYBALT</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.81">I am for you.</a><br>
<p><i>Drawing</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech29"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.82">Gentle Mercutio, put thy rapier up.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech30"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.83">Come, sir, your passado.</a><br>
<p><i>They fight</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech31"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.84">Draw, Benvolio; beat down their weapons.</a><br>
<a name="3.1.85">Gentlemen, for shame, forbear this outrage!</a><br>
<a name="3.1.86">Tybalt, Mercutio, the prince expressly hath</a><br>
<a name="3.1.87">Forbidden bandying in Verona streets:</a><br>
<a name="3.1.88">Hold, Tybalt! good Mercutio!</a><br>
<p><i>TYBALT under ROMEO's arm stabs MERCUTIO, and flies with his followers</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech32"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.89">I am hurt.</a><br>
<a name="3.1.90">A plague o' both your houses! I am sped.</a><br>
<a name="3.1.91">Is he gone, and hath nothing?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech33"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.92">What, art thou hurt?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech34"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.93">Ay, ay, a scratch, a scratch; marry, 'tis enough.</a><br>
<a name="3.1.94">Where is my page? Go, villain, fetch a surgeon.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit Page</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech35"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.95">Courage, man; the hurt cannot be much.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech36"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.96">No, 'tis not so deep as a well, nor so wide as a</a><br>
<a name="3.1.97">church-door; but 'tis enough,'twill serve: ask for</a><br>
<a name="3.1.98">me to-morrow, and you shall find me a grave man. I</a><br>
<a name="3.1.99">am peppered, I warrant, for this world. A plague o'</a><br>
<a name="3.1.100">both your houses! 'Zounds, a dog, a rat, a mouse, a</a><br>
<a name="3.1.101">cat, to scratch a man to death! a braggart, a</a><br>
<a name="3.1.102">rogue, a villain, that fights by the book of</a><br>
<a name="3.1.103">arithmetic! Why the devil came you between us? I</a><br>
<a name="3.1.104">was hurt under your arm.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech37"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.105">I thought all for the best.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech38"><b>MERCUTIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.106">Help me into some house, Benvolio,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.107">Or I shall faint. A plague o' both your houses!</a><br>
<a name="3.1.108">They have made worms' meat of me: I have it,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.109">And soundly too: your houses!</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt MERCUTIO and BENVOLIO</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech39"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.110">This gentleman, the prince's near ally,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.111">My very friend, hath got his mortal hurt</a><br>
<a name="3.1.112">In my behalf; my reputation stain'd</a><br>
<a name="3.1.113">With Tybalt's slander,--Tybalt, that an hour</a><br>
<a name="3.1.114">Hath been my kinsman! O sweet Juliet,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.115">Thy beauty hath made me effeminate</a><br>
<a name="3.1.116">And in my temper soften'd valour's steel!</a><br>
<p><i>Re-enter BENVOLIO</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech40"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.117">O Romeo, Romeo, brave Mercutio's dead!</a><br>
<a name="3.1.118">That gallant spirit hath aspired the clouds,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.119">Which too untimely here did scorn the earth.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech41"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.120">This day's black fate on more days doth depend;</a><br>
<a name="3.1.121">This but begins the woe, others must end.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech42"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.122">Here comes the furious Tybalt back again.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech43"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.123">Alive, in triumph! and Mercutio slain!</a><br>
<a name="3.1.124">Away to heaven, respective lenity,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.125">And fire-eyed fury be my conduct now!</a><br>
<p><i>Re-enter TYBALT</i></p>
<a name="3.1.126">Now, Tybalt, take the villain back again,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.127">That late thou gavest me; for Mercutio's soul</a><br>
<a name="3.1.128">Is but a little way above our heads,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.129">Staying for thine to keep him company:</a><br>
<a name="3.1.130">Either thou, or I, or both, must go with him.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech44"><b>TYBALT</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.131">Thou, wretched boy, that didst consort him here,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.132">Shalt with him hence.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech45"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.133">This shall determine that.</a><br>
<p><i>They fight; TYBALT falls</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech46"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.134">Romeo, away, be gone!</a><br>
<a name="3.1.135">The citizens are up, and Tybalt slain.</a><br>
<a name="3.1.136">Stand not amazed: the prince will doom thee death,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.137">If thou art taken: hence, be gone, away!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech47"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.138">O, I am fortune's fool!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech48"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.139">Why dost thou stay?</a><br>
<p><i>Exit ROMEO</i></p>
<p><i>Enter Citizens,  &amp; c</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech49"><b>First Citizen</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.140">Which way ran he that kill'd Mercutio?</a><br>
<a name="3.1.141">Tybalt, that murderer, which way ran he?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech50"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.142">There lies that Tybalt.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech51"><b>First Citizen</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.143">Up, sir, go with me;</a><br>
<a name="3.1.144">I charge thee in the princes name, obey.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter Prince, attended; MONTAGUE, CAPULET, their Wives, and others</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech52"><b>PRINCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.145">Where are the vile beginners of this fray?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech53"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.146">O noble prince, I can discover all</a><br>
<a name="3.1.147">The unlucky manage of this fatal brawl:</a><br>
<a name="3.1.148">There lies the man, slain by young Romeo,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.149">That slew thy kinsman, brave Mercutio.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech54"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.150">Tybalt, my cousin! O my brother's child!</a><br>
<a name="3.1.151">O prince! O cousin! husband! O, the blood is spilt</a><br>
<a name="3.1.152">O my dear kinsman! Prince, as thou art true,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.153">For blood of ours, shed blood of Montague.</a><br>
<a name="3.1.154">O cousin, cousin!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech55"><b>PRINCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.155">Benvolio, who began this bloody fray?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech56"><b>BENVOLIO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.156">Tybalt, here slain, whom Romeo's hand did slay;</a><br>
<a name="3.1.157">Romeo that spoke him fair, bade him bethink</a><br>
<a name="3.1.158">How nice the quarrel was, and urged withal</a><br>
<a name="3.1.159">Your high displeasure: all this uttered</a><br>
<a name="3.1.160">With gentle breath, calm look, knees humbly bow'd,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.161">Could not take truce with the unruly spleen</a><br>
<a name="3.1.162">Of Tybalt deaf to peace, but that he tilts</a><br>
<a name="3.1.163">With piercing steel at bold Mercutio's breast,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.164">Who all as hot, turns deadly point to point,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.165">And, with a martial scorn, with one hand beats</a><br>
<a name="3.1.166">Cold death aside, and with the other sends</a><br>
<a name="3.1.167">It back to Tybalt, whose dexterity,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.168">Retorts it: Romeo he cries aloud,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.169">'Hold, friends! friends, part!' and, swifter than</a><br>
<a name="3.1.170">his tongue,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.171">His agile arm beats down their fatal points,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.172">And 'twixt them rushes; underneath whose arm</a><br>
<a name="3.1.173">An envious thrust from Tybalt hit the life</a><br>
<a name="3.1.174">Of stout Mercutio, and then Tybalt fled;</a><br>
<a name="3.1.175">But by and by comes back to Romeo,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.176">Who had but newly entertain'd revenge,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.177">And to 't they go like lightning, for, ere I</a><br>
<a name="3.1.178">Could draw to part them, was stout Tybalt slain.</a><br>
<a name="3.1.179">And, as he fell, did Romeo turn and fly.</a><br>
<a name="3.1.180">This is the truth, or let Benvolio die.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech57"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.181">He is a kinsman to the Montague;</a><br>
<a name="3.1.182">Affection makes him false; he speaks not true:</a><br>
<a name="3.1.183">Some twenty of them fought in this black strife,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.184">And all those twenty could but kill one life.</a><br>
<a name="3.1.185">I beg for justice, which thou, prince, must give;</a><br>
<a name="3.1.186">Romeo slew Tybalt, Romeo must not live.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech58"><b>PRINCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.187">Romeo slew him, he slew Mercutio;</a><br>
<a name="3.1.188">Who now the price of his dear blood doth owe?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech59"><b>MONTAGUE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.189">Not Romeo, prince, he was Mercutio's friend;</a><br>
<a name="3.1.190">His fault concludes but what the law should end,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.191">The life of Tybalt.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech60"><b>PRINCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.1.192">And for that offence</a><br>
<a name="3.1.193">Immediately we do exile him hence:</a><br>
<a name="3.1.194">I have an interest in your hate's proceeding,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.195">My blood for your rude brawls doth lie a-bleeding;</a><br>
<a name="3.1.196">But I'll amerce you with so strong a fine</a><br>
<a name="3.1.197">That you shall all repent the loss of mine:</a><br>
<a name="3.1.198">I will be deaf to pleading and excuses;</a><br>
<a name="3.1.199">Nor tears nor prayers shall purchase out abuses:</a><br>
<a name="3.1.200">Therefore use none: let Romeo hence in haste,</a><br>
<a name="3.1.201">Else, when he's found, that hour is his last.</a><br>
<a name="3.1.202">Bear hence this body and attend our will:</a><br>
<a name="3.1.203">Mercy but murders, pardoning those that kill.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE II. Capulet's orchard.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter JULIET</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.1">Gallop apace, you fiery-footed steeds,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.2">Towards Phoebus' lodging: such a wagoner</a><br>
<a name="3.2.3">As Phaethon would whip you to the west,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.4">And bring in cloudy night immediately.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.5">Spread thy close curtain, love-performing night,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.6">That runaway's eyes may wink and Romeo</a><br>
<a name="3.2.7">Leap to these arms, untalk'd of and unseen.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.8">Lovers can see to do their amorous rites</a><br>
<a name="3.2.9">By their own beauties; or, if love be blind,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.10">It best agrees with night. Come, civil night,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.11">Thou sober-suited matron, all in black,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.12">And learn me how to lose a winning match,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.13">Play'd for a pair of stainless maidenhoods:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.14">Hood my unmann'd blood, bating in my cheeks,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.15">With thy black mantle; till strange love, grown bold,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.16">Think true love acted simple modesty.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.17">Come, night; come, Romeo; come, thou day in night;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.18">For thou wilt lie upon the wings of night</a><br>
<a name="3.2.19">Whiter than new snow on a raven's back.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.20">Come, gentle night, come, loving, black-brow'd night,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.21">Give me my Romeo; and, when he shall die,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.22">Take him and cut him out in little stars,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.23">And he will make the face of heaven so fine</a><br>
<a name="3.2.24">That all the world will be in love with night</a><br>
<a name="3.2.25">And pay no worship to the garish sun.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.26">O, I have bought the mansion of a love,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.27">But not possess'd it, and, though I am sold,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.28">Not yet enjoy'd: so tedious is this day</a><br>
<a name="3.2.29">As is the night before some festival</a><br>
<a name="3.2.30">To an impatient child that hath new robes</a><br>
<a name="3.2.31">And may not wear them. O, here comes my nurse,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.32">And she brings news; and every tongue that speaks</a><br>
<a name="3.2.33">But Romeo's name speaks heavenly eloquence.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter Nurse, with cords</i></p>
<a name="3.2.34">Now, nurse, what news? What hast thou there? the cords</a><br>
<a name="3.2.35">That Romeo bid thee fetch?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.36">Ay, ay, the cords.</a><br>
<p><i>Throws them down</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.37">Ay me! what news? why dost thou wring thy hands?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.38">Ah, well-a-day! he's dead, he's dead, he's dead!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.39">We are undone, lady, we are undone!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.40">Alack the day! he's gone, he's kill'd, he's dead!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.41">Can heaven be so envious?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.42">Romeo can,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.43">Though heaven cannot: O Romeo, Romeo!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.44">Who ever would have thought it? Romeo!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.45">What devil art thou, that dost torment me thus?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.46">This torture should be roar'd in dismal hell.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.47">Hath Romeo slain himself? say thou but 'I,'</a><br>
<a name="3.2.48">And that bare vowel 'I' shall poison more</a><br>
<a name="3.2.49">Than the death-darting eye of cockatrice:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.50">I am not I, if there be such an I;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.51">Or those eyes shut, that make thee answer 'I.'</a><br>
<a name="3.2.52">If he be slain, say 'I'; or if not, no:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.53">Brief sounds determine of my weal or woe.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.54">I saw the wound, I saw it with mine eyes,--</a><br>
<a name="3.2.55">God save the mark!--here on his manly breast:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.56">A piteous corse, a bloody piteous corse;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.57">Pale, pale as ashes, all bedaub'd in blood,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.58">All in gore-blood; I swounded at the sight.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.59">O, break, my heart! poor bankrupt, break at once!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.60">To prison, eyes, ne'er look on liberty!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.61">Vile earth, to earth resign; end motion here;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.62">And thou and Romeo press one heavy bier!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.63">O Tybalt, Tybalt, the best friend I had!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.64">O courteous Tybalt! honest gentleman!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.65">That ever I should live to see thee dead!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.66">What storm is this that blows so contrary?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.67">Is Romeo slaughter'd, and is Tybalt dead?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.68">My dear-loved cousin, and my dearer lord?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.69">Then, dreadful trumpet, sound the general doom!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.70">For who is living, if those two are gone?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.71">Tybalt is gone, and Romeo banished;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.72">Romeo that kill'd him, he is banished.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.73">O God! did Romeo's hand shed Tybalt's blood?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.74">It did, it did; alas the day, it did!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.75">O serpent heart, hid with a flowering face!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.76">Did ever dragon keep so fair a cave?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.77">Beautiful tyrant! fiend angelical!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.78">Dove-feather'd raven! wolvish-ravening lamb!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.79">Despised substance of divinest show!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.80">Just opposite to what thou justly seem'st,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.81">A damned saint, an honourable villain!</a><br>
<a name="3.2.82">O nature, what hadst thou to do in hell,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.83">When thou didst bower the spirit of a fiend</a><br>
<a name="3.2.84">In moral paradise of such sweet flesh?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.85">Was ever book containing such vile matter</a><br>
<a name="3.2.86">So fairly bound? O that deceit should dwell</a><br>
<a name="3.2.87">In such a gorgeous palace!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.88">There's no trust,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.89">No faith, no honesty in men; all perjured,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.90">All forsworn, all naught, all dissemblers.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.91">Ah, where's my man? give me some aqua vitae:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.92">These griefs, these woes, these sorrows make me old.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.93">Shame come to Romeo!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.94">Blister'd be thy tongue</a><br>
<a name="3.2.95">For such a wish! he was not born to shame:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.96">Upon his brow shame is ashamed to sit;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.97">For 'tis a throne where honour may be crown'd</a><br>
<a name="3.2.98">Sole monarch of the universal earth.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.99">O, what a beast was I to chide at him!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.100">Will you speak well of him that kill'd your cousin?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech19"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.101">Shall I speak ill of him that is my husband?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.102">Ah, poor my lord, what tongue shall smooth thy name,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.103">When I, thy three-hours wife, have mangled it?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.104">But, wherefore, villain, didst thou kill my cousin?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.105">That villain cousin would have kill'd my husband:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.106">Back, foolish tears, back to your native spring;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.107">Your tributary drops belong to woe,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.108">Which you, mistaking, offer up to joy.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.109">My husband lives, that Tybalt would have slain;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.110">And Tybalt's dead, that would have slain my husband:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.111">All this is comfort; wherefore weep I then?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.112">Some word there was, worser than Tybalt's death,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.113">That murder'd me: I would forget it fain;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.114">But, O, it presses to my memory,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.115">Like damned guilty deeds to sinners' minds:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.116">'Tybalt is dead, and Romeo--banished;'</a><br>
<a name="3.2.117">That 'banished,' that one word 'banished,'</a><br>
<a name="3.2.118">Hath slain ten thousand Tybalts. Tybalt's death</a><br>
<a name="3.2.119">Was woe enough, if it had ended there:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.120">Or, if sour woe delights in fellowship</a><br>
<a name="3.2.121">And needly will be rank'd with other griefs,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.122">Why follow'd not, when she said 'Tybalt's dead,'</a><br>
<a name="3.2.123">Thy father, or thy mother, nay, or both,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.124">Which modern lamentations might have moved?</a><br>
<a name="3.2.125">But with a rear-ward following Tybalt's death,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.126">'Romeo is banished,' to speak that word,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.127">Is father, mother, Tybalt, Romeo, Juliet,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.128">All slain, all dead. 'Romeo is banished!'</a><br>
<a name="3.2.129">There is no end, no limit, measure, bound,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.130">In that word's death; no words can that woe sound.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.131">Where is my father, and my mother, nurse?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech20"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.132">Weeping and wailing over Tybalt's corse:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.133">Will you go to them? I will bring you thither.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech21"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.134">Wash they his wounds with tears: mine shall be spent,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.135">When theirs are dry, for Romeo's banishment.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.136">Take up those cords: poor ropes, you are beguiled,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.137">Both you and I; for Romeo is exiled:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.138">He made you for a highway to my bed;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.139">But I, a maid, die maiden-widowed.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.140">Come, cords, come, nurse; I'll to my wedding-bed;</a><br>
<a name="3.2.141">And death, not Romeo, take my maidenhead!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech22"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.142">Hie to your chamber: I'll find Romeo</a><br>
<a name="3.2.143">To comfort you: I wot well where he is.</a><br>
<a name="3.2.144">Hark ye, your Romeo will be here at night:</a><br>
<a name="3.2.145">I'll to him; he is hid at Laurence' cell.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech23"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.2.146">O, find him! give this ring to my true knight,</a><br>
<a name="3.2.147">And bid him come to take his last farewell.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE III. Friar Laurence's cell.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter FRIAR LAURENCE</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.1">Romeo, come forth; come forth, thou fearful man:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.2">Affliction is enamour'd of thy parts,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.3">And thou art wedded to calamity.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter ROMEO</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.4">Father, what news? what is the prince's doom?</a><br>
<a name="3.3.5">What sorrow craves acquaintance at my hand,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.6">That I yet know not?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.7">Too familiar</a><br>
<a name="3.3.8">Is my dear son with such sour company:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.9">I bring thee tidings of the prince's doom.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.10">What less than dooms-day is the prince's doom?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.11">A gentler judgment vanish'd from his lips,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.12">Not body's death, but body's banishment.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.13">Ha, banishment! be merciful, say 'death;'</a><br>
<a name="3.3.14">For exile hath more terror in his look,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.15">Much more than death: do not say 'banishment.'</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.16">Hence from Verona art thou banished:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.17">Be patient, for the world is broad and wide.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.18">There is no world without Verona walls,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.19">But purgatory, torture, hell itself.</a><br>
<a name="3.3.20">Hence-banished is banish'd from the world,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.21">And world's exile is death: then banished,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.22">Is death mis-term'd: calling death banishment,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.23">Thou cutt'st my head off with a golden axe,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.24">And smilest upon the stroke that murders me.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.25">O deadly sin! O rude unthankfulness!</a><br>
<a name="3.3.26">Thy fault our law calls death; but the kind prince,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.27">Taking thy part, hath rush'd aside the law,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.28">And turn'd that black word death to banishment:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.29">This is dear mercy, and thou seest it not.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.30">'Tis torture, and not mercy: heaven is here,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.31">Where Juliet lives; and every cat and dog</a><br>
<a name="3.3.32">And little mouse, every unworthy thing,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.33">Live here in heaven and may look on her;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.34">But Romeo may not: more validity,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.35">More honourable state, more courtship lives</a><br>
<a name="3.3.36">In carrion-flies than Romeo: they my seize</a><br>
<a name="3.3.37">On the white wonder of dear Juliet's hand</a><br>
<a name="3.3.38">And steal immortal blessing from her lips,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.39">Who even in pure and vestal modesty,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.40">Still blush, as thinking their own kisses sin;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.41">But Romeo may not; he is banished:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.42">Flies may do this, but I from this must fly:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.43">They are free men, but I am banished.</a><br>
<a name="3.3.44">And say'st thou yet that exile is not death?</a><br>
<a name="3.3.45">Hadst thou no poison mix'd, no sharp-ground knife,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.46">No sudden mean of death, though ne'er so mean,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.47">But 'banished' to kill me?--'banished'?</a><br>
