<a name="3.3.48">O friar, the damned use that word in hell;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.49">Howlings attend it: how hast thou the heart,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.50">Being a divine, a ghostly confessor,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.51">A sin-absolver, and my friend profess'd,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.52">To mangle me with that word 'banished'?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.53">Thou fond mad man, hear me but speak a word.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.54">O, thou wilt speak again of banishment.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.55">I'll give thee armour to keep off that word:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.56">Adversity's sweet milk, philosophy,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.57">To comfort thee, though thou art banished.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.58">Yet 'banished'? Hang up philosophy!</a><br>
<a name="3.3.59">Unless philosophy can make a Juliet,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.60">Displant a town, reverse a prince's doom,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.61">It helps not, it prevails not: talk no more.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.62">O, then I see that madmen have no ears.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.63">How should they, when that wise men have no eyes?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.64">Let me dispute with thee of thy estate.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.65">Thou canst not speak of that thou dost not feel:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.66">Wert thou as young as I, Juliet thy love,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.67">An hour but married, Tybalt murdered,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.68">Doting like me and like me banished,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.69">Then mightst thou speak, then mightst thou tear thy hair,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.70">And fall upon the ground, as I do now,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.71">Taking the measure of an unmade grave.</a><br>
<p><i>Knocking within</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech19"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.72">Arise; one knocks; good Romeo, hide thyself.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech20"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.73">Not I; unless the breath of heartsick groans,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.74">Mist-like, infold me from the search of eyes.</a><br>
<p><i>Knocking</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech21"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.75">Hark, how they knock! Who's there? Romeo, arise;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.76">Thou wilt be taken. Stay awhile! Stand up;</a><br>
<p><i>Knocking</i></p>
<a name="3.3.77">Run to my study. By and by! God's will,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.78">What simpleness is this! I come, I come!</a><br>
<p><i>Knocking</i></p>
<a name="3.3.79">Who knocks so hard? whence come you? what's your will?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech22"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.80">[Within]  Let me come in, and you shall know</a><br>
<a name="3.3.81">my errand;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.82">I come from Lady Juliet.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech23"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.83">Welcome, then.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter Nurse</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech24"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.84">O holy friar, O, tell me, holy friar,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.85">Where is my lady's lord, where's Romeo?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech25"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.86">There on the ground, with his own tears made drunk.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech26"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.87">O, he is even in my mistress' case,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.88">Just in her case! O woful sympathy!</a><br>
<a name="3.3.89">Piteous predicament! Even so lies she,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.90">Blubbering and weeping, weeping and blubbering.</a><br>
<a name="3.3.91">Stand up, stand up; stand, and you be a man:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.92">For Juliet's sake, for her sake, rise and stand;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.93">Why should you fall into so deep an O?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech27"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.94">Nurse!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech28"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.95">Ah sir! ah sir! Well, death's the end of all.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech29"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.96">Spakest thou of Juliet? how is it with her?</a><br>
<a name="3.3.97">Doth she not think me an old murderer,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.98">Now I have stain'd the childhood of our joy</a><br>
<a name="3.3.99">With blood removed but little from her own?</a><br>
<a name="3.3.100">Where is she? and how doth she? and what says</a><br>
<a name="3.3.101">My conceal'd lady to our cancell'd love?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech30"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.102">O, she says nothing, sir, but weeps and weeps;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.103">And now falls on her bed; and then starts up,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.104">And Tybalt calls; and then on Romeo cries,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.105">And then down falls again.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech31"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.106">As if that name,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.107">Shot from the deadly level of a gun,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.108">Did murder her; as that name's cursed hand</a><br>
<a name="3.3.109">Murder'd her kinsman. O, tell me, friar, tell me,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.110">In what vile part of this anatomy</a><br>
<a name="3.3.111">Doth my name lodge? tell me, that I may sack</a><br>
<a name="3.3.112">The hateful mansion.</a><br>
<p><i>Drawing his sword</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech32"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.113">Hold thy desperate hand:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.114">Art thou a man? thy form cries out thou art:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.115">Thy tears are womanish; thy wild acts denote</a><br>
<a name="3.3.116">The unreasonable fury of a beast:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.117">Unseemly woman in a seeming man!</a><br>
<a name="3.3.118">Or ill-beseeming beast in seeming both!</a><br>
<a name="3.3.119">Thou hast amazed me: by my holy order,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.120">I thought thy disposition better temper'd.</a><br>
<a name="3.3.121">Hast thou slain Tybalt? wilt thou slay thyself?</a><br>
<a name="3.3.122">And stay thy lady too that lives in thee,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.123">By doing damned hate upon thyself?</a><br>
<a name="3.3.124">Why rail'st thou on thy birth, the heaven, and earth?</a><br>
<a name="3.3.125">Since birth, and heaven, and earth, all three do meet</a><br>
<a name="3.3.126">In thee at once; which thou at once wouldst lose.</a><br>
<a name="3.3.127">Fie, fie, thou shamest thy shape, thy love, thy wit;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.128">Which, like a usurer, abound'st in all,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.129">And usest none in that true use indeed</a><br>
<a name="3.3.130">Which should bedeck thy shape, thy love, thy wit:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.131">Thy noble shape is but a form of wax,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.132">Digressing from the valour of a man;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.133">Thy dear love sworn but hollow perjury,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.134">Killing that love which thou hast vow'd to cherish;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.135">Thy wit, that ornament to shape and love,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.136">Misshapen in the conduct of them both,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.137">Like powder in a skitless soldier's flask,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.138">Is set afire by thine own ignorance,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.139">And thou dismember'd with thine own defence.</a><br>
<a name="3.3.140">What, rouse thee, man! thy Juliet is alive,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.141">For whose dear sake thou wast but lately dead;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.142">There art thou happy: Tybalt would kill thee,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.143">But thou slew'st Tybalt; there are thou happy too:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.144">The law that threaten'd death becomes thy friend</a><br>
<a name="3.3.145">And turns it to exile; there art thou happy:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.146">A pack of blessings lights up upon thy back;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.147">Happiness courts thee in her best array;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.148">But, like a misbehaved and sullen wench,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.149">Thou pout'st upon thy fortune and thy love:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.150">Take heed, take heed, for such die miserable.</a><br>
<a name="3.3.151">Go, get thee to thy love, as was decreed,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.152">Ascend her chamber, hence and comfort her:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.153">But look thou stay not till the watch be set,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.154">For then thou canst not pass to Mantua;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.155">Where thou shalt live, till we can find a time</a><br>
<a name="3.3.156">To blaze your marriage, reconcile your friends,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.157">Beg pardon of the prince, and call thee back</a><br>
<a name="3.3.158">With twenty hundred thousand times more joy</a><br>
<a name="3.3.159">Than thou went'st forth in lamentation.</a><br>
<a name="3.3.160">Go before, nurse: commend me to thy lady;</a><br>
<a name="3.3.161">And bid her hasten all the house to bed,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.162">Which heavy sorrow makes them apt unto:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.163">Romeo is coming.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech33"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.164">O Lord, I could have stay'd here all the night</a><br>
<a name="3.3.165">To hear good counsel: O, what learning is!</a><br>
<a name="3.3.166">My lord, I'll tell my lady you will come.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech34"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.167">Do so, and bid my sweet prepare to chide.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech35"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.168">Here, sir, a ring she bid me give you, sir:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.169">Hie you, make haste, for it grows very late.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech36"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.170">How well my comfort is revived by this!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech37"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.171">Go hence; good night; and here stands all your state:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.172">Either be gone before the watch be set,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.173">Or by the break of day disguised from hence:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.174">Sojourn in Mantua; I'll find out your man,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.175">And he shall signify from time to time</a><br>
<a name="3.3.176">Every good hap to you that chances here:</a><br>
<a name="3.3.177">Give me thy hand; 'tis late: farewell; good night.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech38"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.3.178">But that a joy past joy calls out on me,</a><br>
<a name="3.3.179">It were a grief, so brief to part with thee: Farewell.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE IV. A room in Capulet's house.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter CAPULET, LADY CAPULET, and PARIS</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.4.1">Things have fall'n out, sir, so unluckily,</a><br>
<a name="3.4.2">That we have had no time to move our daughter:</a><br>
<a name="3.4.3">Look you, she loved her kinsman Tybalt dearly,</a><br>
<a name="3.4.4">And so did I:--Well, we were born to die.</a><br>
<a name="3.4.5">'Tis very late, she'll not come down to-night:</a><br>
<a name="3.4.6">I promise you, but for your company,</a><br>
<a name="3.4.7">I would have been a-bed an hour ago.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.4.8">These times of woe afford no time to woo.</a><br>
<a name="3.4.9">Madam, good night: commend me to your daughter.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.4.10">I will, and know her mind early to-morrow;</a><br>
<a name="3.4.11">To-night she is mew'd up to her heaviness.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.4.12">Sir Paris, I will make a desperate tender</a><br>
<a name="3.4.13">Of my child's love: I think she will be ruled</a><br>
<a name="3.4.14">In all respects by me; nay, more, I doubt it not.</a><br>
<a name="3.4.15">Wife, go you to her ere you go to bed;</a><br>
<a name="3.4.16">Acquaint her here of my son Paris' love;</a><br>
<a name="3.4.17">And bid her, mark you me, on Wednesday next--</a><br>
<a name="3.4.18">But, soft! what day is this?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.4.19">Monday, my lord,</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.4.20">Monday! ha, ha! Well, Wednesday is too soon,</a><br>
<a name="3.4.21">O' Thursday let it be: o' Thursday, tell her,</a><br>
<a name="3.4.22">She shall be married to this noble earl.</a><br>
<a name="3.4.23">Will you be ready? do you like this haste?</a><br>
<a name="3.4.24">We'll keep no great ado,--a friend or two;</a><br>
<a name="3.4.25">For, hark you, Tybalt being slain so late,</a><br>
<a name="3.4.26">It may be thought we held him carelessly,</a><br>
<a name="3.4.27">Being our kinsman, if we revel much:</a><br>
<a name="3.4.28">Therefore we'll have some half a dozen friends,</a><br>
<a name="3.4.29">And there an end. But what say you to Thursday?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.4.30">My lord, I would that Thursday were to-morrow.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.4.31">Well get you gone: o' Thursday be it, then.</a><br>
<a name="3.4.32">Go you to Juliet ere you go to bed,</a><br>
<a name="3.4.33">Prepare her, wife, against this wedding-day.</a><br>
<a name="3.4.34">Farewell, my lord. Light to my chamber, ho!</a><br>
<a name="3.4.35">Afore me! it is so very very late,</a><br>
<a name="3.4.36">That we may call it early by and by.</a><br>
<a name="3.4.37">Good night.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE V. Capulet's orchard.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter ROMEO and JULIET above, at the window</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.1">Wilt thou be gone? it is not yet near day:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.2">It was the nightingale, and not the lark,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.3">That pierced the fearful hollow of thine ear;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.4">Nightly she sings on yon pomegranate-tree:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.5">Believe me, love, it was the nightingale.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.6">It was the lark, the herald of the morn,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.7">No nightingale: look, love, what envious streaks</a><br>
<a name="3.5.8">Do lace the severing clouds in yonder east:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.9">Night's candles are burnt out, and jocund day</a><br>
<a name="3.5.10">Stands tiptoe on the misty mountain tops.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.11">I must be gone and live, or stay and die.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.12">Yon light is not day-light, I know it, I:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.13">It is some meteor that the sun exhales,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.14">To be to thee this night a torch-bearer,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.15">And light thee on thy way to Mantua:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.16">Therefore stay yet; thou need'st not to be gone.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.17">Let me be ta'en, let me be put to death;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.18">I am content, so thou wilt have it so.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.19">I'll say yon grey is not the morning's eye,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.20">'Tis but the pale reflex of Cynthia's brow;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.21">Nor that is not the lark, whose notes do beat</a><br>
<a name="3.5.22">The vaulty heaven so high above our heads:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.23">I have more care to stay than will to go:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.24">Come, death, and welcome! Juliet wills it so.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.25">How is't, my soul? let's talk; it is not day.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.26">It is, it is: hie hence, be gone, away!</a><br>
<a name="3.5.27">It is the lark that sings so out of tune,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.28">Straining harsh discords and unpleasing sharps.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.29">Some say the lark makes sweet division;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.30">This doth not so, for she divideth us:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.31">Some say the lark and loathed toad change eyes,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.32">O, now I would they had changed voices too!</a><br>
<a name="3.5.33">Since arm from arm that voice doth us affray,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.34">Hunting thee hence with hunt's-up to the day,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.35">O, now be gone; more light and light it grows.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.36">More light and light; more dark and dark our woes!</a><br>
<p><i>Enter Nurse, to the chamber</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.37">Madam!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.38">Nurse?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.39">Your lady mother is coming to your chamber:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.40">The day is broke; be wary, look about.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.41">Then, window, let day in, and let life out.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.42">Farewell, farewell! one kiss, and I'll descend.</a><br>
<p><i>He goeth down</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.43">Art thou gone so? love, lord, ay, husband, friend!</a><br>
<a name="3.5.44">I must hear from thee every day in the hour,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.45">For in a minute there are many days:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.46">O, by this count I shall be much in years</a><br>
<a name="3.5.47">Ere I again behold my Romeo!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.48">Farewell!</a><br>
<a name="3.5.49">I will omit no opportunity</a><br>
<a name="3.5.50">That may convey my greetings, love, to thee.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.51">O think'st thou we shall ever meet again?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.52">I doubt it not; and all these woes shall serve</a><br>
<a name="3.5.53">For sweet discourses in our time to come.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.54">O God, I have an ill-divining soul!</a><br>
<a name="3.5.55">Methinks I see thee, now thou art below,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.56">As one dead in the bottom of a tomb:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.57">Either my eyesight fails, or thou look'st pale.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.58">And trust me, love, in my eye so do you:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.59">Dry sorrow drinks our blood. Adieu, adieu!</a><br>
<p><i>Exit</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.60">O fortune, fortune! all men call thee fickle:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.61">If thou art fickle, what dost thou with him.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.62">That is renown'd for faith? Be fickle, fortune;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.63">For then, I hope, thou wilt not keep him long,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.64">But send him back.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech19"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.65">[Within]         Ho, daughter! are you up?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech20"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.66">Who is't that calls? is it my lady mother?</a><br>
<a name="3.5.67">Is she not down so late, or up so early?</a><br>
<a name="3.5.68">What unaccustom'd cause procures her hither?</a><br>
<p><i>Enter LADY CAPULET</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech21"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.69">Why, how now, Juliet!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech22"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.70">Madam, I am not well.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech23"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.71">Evermore weeping for your cousin's death?</a><br>
<a name="3.5.72">What, wilt thou wash him from his grave with tears?</a><br>
<a name="3.5.73">An if thou couldst, thou couldst not make him live;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.74">Therefore, have done: some grief shows much of love;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.75">But much of grief shows still some want of wit.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech24"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.76">Yet let me weep for such a feeling loss.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech25"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.77">So shall you feel the loss, but not the friend</a><br>
<a name="3.5.78">Which you weep for.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech26"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.79">Feeling so the loss,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.80">Cannot choose but ever weep the friend.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech27"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.81">Well, girl, thou weep'st not so much for his death,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.82">As that the villain lives which slaughter'd him.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech28"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.83">What villain madam?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech29"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.84">That same villain, Romeo.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech30"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.85">[Aside]  Villain and he be many miles asunder.--</a><br>
<a name="3.5.86">God Pardon him! I do, with all my heart;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.87">And yet no man like he doth grieve my heart.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech31"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.88">That is, because the traitor murderer lives.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech32"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.89">Ay, madam, from the reach of these my hands:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.90">Would none but I might venge my cousin's death!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech33"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.91">We will have vengeance for it, fear thou not:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.92">Then weep no more. I'll send to one in Mantua,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.93">Where that same banish'd runagate doth live,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.94">Shall give him such an unaccustom'd dram,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.95">That he shall soon keep Tybalt company:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.96">And then, I hope, thou wilt be satisfied.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech34"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.97">Indeed, I never shall be satisfied</a><br>
<a name="3.5.98">With Romeo, till I behold him--dead--</a><br>
<a name="3.5.99">Is my poor heart for a kinsman vex'd.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.100">Madam, if you could find out but a man</a><br>
<a name="3.5.101">To bear a poison, I would temper it;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.102">That Romeo should, upon receipt thereof,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.103">Soon sleep in quiet. O, how my heart abhors</a><br>
<a name="3.5.104">To hear him named, and cannot come to him.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.105">To wreak the love I bore my cousin</a><br>
<a name="3.5.106">Upon his body that slaughter'd him!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech35"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.107">Find thou the means, and I'll find such a man.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.108">But now I'll tell thee joyful tidings, girl.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech36"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.109">And joy comes well in such a needy time:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.110">What are they, I beseech your ladyship?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech37"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.111">Well, well, thou hast a careful father, child;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.112">One who, to put thee from thy heaviness,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.113">Hath sorted out a sudden day of joy,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.114">That thou expect'st not nor I look'd not for.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech38"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.115">Madam, in happy time, what day is that?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech39"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.116">Marry, my child, early next Thursday morn,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.117">The gallant, young and noble gentleman,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.118">The County Paris, at Saint Peter's Church,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.119">Shall happily make thee there a joyful bride.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech40"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.120">Now, by Saint Peter's Church and Peter too,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.121">He shall not make me there a joyful bride.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.122">I wonder at this haste; that I must wed</a><br>
<a name="3.5.123">Ere he, that should be husband, comes to woo.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.124">I pray you, tell my lord and father, madam,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.125">I will not marry yet; and, when I do, I swear,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.126">It shall be Romeo, whom you know I hate,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.127">Rather than Paris. These are news indeed!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech41"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.128">Here comes your father; tell him so yourself,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.129">And see how he will take it at your hands.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter CAPULET and Nurse</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech42"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.130">When the sun sets, the air doth drizzle dew;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.131">But for the sunset of my brother's son</a><br>
<a name="3.5.132">It rains downright.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.133">How now! a conduit, girl? what, still in tears?</a><br>
<a name="3.5.134">Evermore showering? In one little body</a><br>
<a name="3.5.135">Thou counterfeit'st a bark, a sea, a wind;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.136">For still thy eyes, which I may call the sea,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.137">Do ebb and flow with tears; the bark thy body is,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.138">Sailing in this salt flood; the winds, thy sighs;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.139">Who, raging with thy tears, and they with them,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.140">Without a sudden calm, will overset</a><br>
<a name="3.5.141">Thy tempest-tossed body. How now, wife!</a><br>
<a name="3.5.142">Have you deliver'd to her our decree?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech43"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.143">Ay, sir; but she will none, she gives you thanks.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.144">I would the fool were married to her grave!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech44"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.145">Soft! take me with you, take me with you, wife.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.146">How! will she none? doth she not give us thanks?</a><br>
<a name="3.5.147">Is she not proud? doth she not count her blest,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.148">Unworthy as she is, that we have wrought</a><br>
<a name="3.5.149">So worthy a gentleman to be her bridegroom?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech45"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.150">Not proud, you have; but thankful, that you have:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.151">Proud can I never be of what I hate;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.152">But thankful even for hate, that is meant love.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech46"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.153">How now, how now, chop-logic! What is this?</a><br>
<a name="3.5.154">'Proud,' and 'I thank you,' and 'I thank you not;'</a><br>
<a name="3.5.155">And yet 'not proud,' mistress minion, you,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.156">Thank me no thankings, nor, proud me no prouds,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.157">But fettle your fine joints 'gainst Thursday next,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.158">To go with Paris to Saint Peter's Church,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.159">Or I will drag thee on a hurdle thither.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.160">Out, you green-sickness carrion! out, you baggage!</a><br>
<a name="3.5.161">You tallow-face!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech47"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.162">                  Fie, fie! what, are you mad?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech48"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.163">Good father, I beseech you on my knees,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.164">Hear me with patience but to speak a word.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech49"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.165">Hang thee, young baggage! disobedient wretch!</a><br>
<a name="3.5.166">I tell thee what: get thee to church o' Thursday,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.167">Or never after look me in the face:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.168">Speak not, reply not, do not answer me;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.169">My fingers itch. Wife, we scarce thought us blest</a><br>
<a name="3.5.170">That God had lent us but this only child;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.171">But now I see this one is one too much,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.172">And that we have a curse in having her:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.173">Out on her, hilding!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech50"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.174">God in heaven bless her!</a><br>
<a name="3.5.175">You are to blame, my lord, to rate her so.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech51"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.176">And why, my lady wisdom? hold your tongue,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.177">Good prudence; smatter with your gossips, go.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech52"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.178">I speak no treason.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech53"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.179">O, God ye god-den.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech54"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.180">May not one speak?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech55"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.181">                  Peace, you mumbling fool!</a><br>
<a name="3.5.182">Utter your gravity o'er a gossip's bowl;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.183">For here we need it not.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech56"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.184">You are too hot.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech57"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.185">God's bread! it makes me mad:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.186">Day, night, hour, tide, time, work, play,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.187">Alone, in company, still my care hath been</a><br>
<a name="3.5.188">To have her match'd: and having now provided</a><br>
<a name="3.5.189">A gentleman of noble parentage,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.190">Of fair demesnes, youthful, and nobly train'd,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.191">Stuff'd, as they say, with honourable parts,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.192">Proportion'd as one's thought would wish a man;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.193">And then to have a wretched puling fool,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.194">A whining mammet, in her fortune's tender,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.195">To answer 'I'll not wed; I cannot love,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.196">I am too young; I pray you, pardon me.'</a><br>
<a name="3.5.197">But, as you will not wed, I'll pardon you:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.198">Graze where you will you shall not house with me:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.199">Look to't, think on't, I do not use to jest.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.200">Thursday is near; lay hand on heart, advise:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.201">An you be mine, I'll give you to my friend;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.202">And you be not, hang, beg, starve, die in</a><br>
<a name="3.5.203">the streets,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.204">For, by my soul, I'll ne'er acknowledge thee,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.205">Nor what is mine shall never do thee good:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.206">Trust to't, bethink you; I'll not be forsworn.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech58"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.207">Is there no pity sitting in the clouds,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.208">That sees into the bottom of my grief?</a><br>
<a name="3.5.209">O, sweet my mother, cast me not away!</a><br>
<a name="3.5.210">Delay this marriage for a month, a week;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.211">Or, if you do not, make the bridal bed</a><br>
<a name="3.5.212">In that dim monument where Tybalt lies.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech59"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.213">Talk not to me, for I'll not speak a word:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.214">Do as thou wilt, for I have done with thee.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech60"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.215">O God!--O nurse, how shall this be prevented?</a><br>
<a name="3.5.216">My husband is on earth, my faith in heaven;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.217">How shall that faith return again to earth,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.218">Unless that husband send it me from heaven</a><br>
<a name="3.5.219">By leaving earth? comfort me, counsel me.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.220">Alack, alack, that heaven should practise stratagems</a><br>
<a name="3.5.221">Upon so soft a subject as myself!</a><br>
<a name="3.5.222">What say'st thou? hast thou not a word of joy?</a><br>
<a name="3.5.223">Some comfort, nurse.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech61"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.224">Faith, here it is.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.225">Romeo is banish'd; and all the world to nothing,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.226">That he dares ne'er come back to challenge you;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.227">Or, if he do, it needs must be by stealth.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.228">Then, since the case so stands as now it doth,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.229">I think it best you married with the county.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.230">O, he's a lovely gentleman!</a><br>
<a name="3.5.231">Romeo's a dishclout to him: an eagle, madam,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.232">Hath not so green, so quick, so fair an eye</a><br>
<a name="3.5.233">As Paris hath. Beshrew my very heart,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.234">I think you are happy in this second match,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.235">For it excels your first: or if it did not,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.236">Your first is dead; or 'twere as good he were,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.237">As living here and you no use of him.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech62"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.238">Speakest thou from thy heart?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech63"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.239">And from my soul too;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.240">Or else beshrew them both.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech64"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.241">Amen!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech65"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.242">What?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech66"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.243">Well, thou hast comforted me marvellous much.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.244">Go in: and tell my lady I am gone,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.245">Having displeased my father, to Laurence' cell,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.246">To make confession and to be absolved.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech67"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.247">Marry, I will; and this is wisely done.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech68"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="3.5.248">Ancient damnation! O most wicked fiend!</a><br>
<a name="3.5.249">Is it more sin to wish me thus forsworn,</a><br>
<a name="3.5.250">Or to dispraise my lord with that same tongue</a><br>
<a name="3.5.251">Which she hath praised him with above compare</a><br>
<a name="3.5.252">So many thousand times? Go, counsellor;</a><br>
<a name="3.5.253">Thou and my bosom henceforth shall be twain.</a><br>
<a name="3.5.254">I'll to the friar, to know his remedy:</a><br>
<a name="3.5.255">If all else fail, myself have power to die.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit</i></p>
</blockquote><p>
</p><h3>ACT IV</h3>
<h3>SCENE I. Friar Laurence's cell.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter FRIAR LAURENCE and PARIS</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.1">On Thursday, sir? the time is very short.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.2">My father Capulet will have it so;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.3">And I am nothing slow to slack his haste.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.4">You say you do not know the lady's mind:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.5">Uneven is the course, I like it not.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.6">Immoderately she weeps for Tybalt's death,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.7">And therefore have I little talk'd of love;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.8">For Venus smiles not in a house of tears.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.9">Now, sir, her father counts it dangerous</a><br>
<a name="4.1.10">That she doth give her sorrow so much sway,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.11">And in his wisdom hastes our marriage,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.12">To stop the inundation of her tears;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.13">Which, too much minded by herself alone,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.14">May be put from her by society:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.15">Now do you know the reason of this haste.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.16">[Aside]  I would I knew not why it should be slow'd.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.17">Look, sir, here comes the lady towards my cell.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter JULIET</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.18">Happily met, my lady and my wife!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.19">That may be, sir, when I may be a wife.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.20">That may be must be, love, on Thursday next.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.21">What must be shall be.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.22">That's a certain text.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.23">Come you to make confession to this father?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.24">To answer that, I should confess to you.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.25">Do not deny to him that you love me.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.26">I will confess to you that I love him.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.27">So will ye, I am sure, that you love me.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.28">If I do so, it will be of more price,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.29">Being spoke behind your back, than to your face.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.30">Poor soul, thy face is much abused with tears.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.31">The tears have got small victory by that;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.32">For it was bad enough before their spite.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech19"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.33">Thou wrong'st it, more than tears, with that report.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech20"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.34">That is no slander, sir, which is a truth;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.35">And what I spake, I spake it to my face.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech21"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.36">Thy face is mine, and thou hast slander'd it.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech22"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.37">It may be so, for it is not mine own.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.38">Are you at leisure, holy father, now;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.39">Or shall I come to you at evening mass?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech23"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.40">My leisure serves me, pensive daughter, now.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.41">My lord, we must entreat the time alone.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech24"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.42">God shield I should disturb devotion!</a><br>
<a name="4.1.43">Juliet, on Thursday early will I rouse ye:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.44">Till then, adieu; and keep this holy kiss.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech25"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.45">O shut the door! and when thou hast done so,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.46">Come weep with me; past hope, past cure, past help!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech26"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.47">Ah, Juliet, I already know thy grief;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.48">It strains me past the compass of my wits:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.49">I hear thou must, and nothing may prorogue it,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.50">On Thursday next be married to this county.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech27"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.51">Tell me not, friar, that thou hear'st of this,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.52">Unless thou tell me how I may prevent it:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.53">If, in thy wisdom, thou canst give no help,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.54">Do thou but call my resolution wise,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.55">And with this knife I'll help it presently.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.56">God join'd my heart and Romeo's, thou our hands;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.57">And ere this hand, by thee to Romeo seal'd,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.58">Shall be the label to another deed,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.59">Or my true heart with treacherous revolt</a><br>
<a name="4.1.60">Turn to another, this shall slay them both:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.61">Therefore, out of thy long-experienced time,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.62">Give me some present counsel, or, behold,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.63">'Twixt my extremes and me this bloody knife</a><br>
<a name="4.1.64">Shall play the umpire, arbitrating that</a><br>
<a name="4.1.65">Which the commission of thy years and art</a><br>
<a name="4.1.66">Could to no issue of true honour bring.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.67">Be not so long to speak; I long to die,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.68">If what thou speak'st speak not of remedy.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech28"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.69">Hold, daughter: I do spy a kind of hope,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.70">Which craves as desperate an execution.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.71">As that is desperate which we would prevent.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.72">If, rather than to marry County Paris,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.73">Thou hast the strength of will to slay thyself,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.74">Then is it likely thou wilt undertake</a><br>
<a name="4.1.75">A thing like death to chide away this shame,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.76">That copest with death himself to scape from it:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.77">And, if thou darest, I'll give thee remedy.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech29"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.78">O, bid me leap, rather than marry Paris,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.79">From off the battlements of yonder tower;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.80">Or walk in thievish ways; or bid me lurk</a><br>
<a name="4.1.81">Where serpents are; chain me with roaring bears;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.82">Or shut me nightly in a charnel-house,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.83">O'er-cover'd quite with dead men's rattling bones,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.84">With reeky shanks and yellow chapless skulls;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.85">Or bid me go into a new-made grave</a><br>
<a name="4.1.86">And hide me with a dead man in his shroud;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.87">Things that, to hear them told, have made me tremble;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.88">And I will do it without fear or doubt,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.89">To live an unstain'd wife to my sweet love.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech30"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.90">Hold, then; go home, be merry, give consent</a><br>
<a name="4.1.91">To marry Paris: Wednesday is to-morrow:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.92">To-morrow night look that thou lie alone;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.93">Let not thy nurse lie with thee in thy chamber:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.94">Take thou this vial, being then in bed,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.95">And this distilled liquor drink thou off;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.96">When presently through all thy veins shall run</a><br>
<a name="4.1.97">A cold and drowsy humour, for no pulse</a><br>
<a name="4.1.98">Shall keep his native progress, but surcease:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.99">No warmth, no breath, shall testify thou livest;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.100">The roses in thy lips and cheeks shall fade</a><br>
<a name="4.1.101">To paly ashes, thy eyes' windows fall,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.102">Like death, when he shuts up the day of life;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.103">Each part, deprived of supple government,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.104">Shall, stiff and stark and cold, appear like death:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.105">And in this borrow'd likeness of shrunk death</a><br>
<a name="4.1.106">Thou shalt continue two and forty hours,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.107">And then awake as from a pleasant sleep.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.108">Now, when the bridegroom in the morning comes</a><br>
<a name="4.1.109">To rouse thee from thy bed, there art thou dead:</a><br>
<a name="4.1.110">Then, as the manner of our country is,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.111">In thy best robes uncover'd on the bier</a><br>
<a name="4.1.112">Thou shalt be borne to that same ancient vault</a><br>
<a name="4.1.113">Where all the kindred of the Capulets lie.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.114">In the mean time, against thou shalt awake,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.115">Shall Romeo by my letters know our drift,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.116">And hither shall he come: and he and I</a><br>
<a name="4.1.117">Will watch thy waking, and that very night</a><br>
<a name="4.1.118">Shall Romeo bear thee hence to Mantua.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.119">And this shall free thee from this present shame;</a><br>
<a name="4.1.120">If no inconstant toy, nor womanish fear,</a><br>
<a name="4.1.121">Abate thy valour in the acting it.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech31"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.122">Give me, give me! O, tell not me of fear!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech32"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.123">Hold; get you gone, be strong and prosperous</a><br>
<a name="4.1.124">In this resolve: I'll send a friar with speed</a><br>
<a name="4.1.125">To Mantua, with my letters to thy lord.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech33"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.1.126">Love give me strength! and strength shall help afford.</a><br>
<a name="4.1.127">Farewell, dear father!</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE II. Hall in Capulet's house.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter CAPULET, LADY  CAPULET, Nurse, and two Servingmen</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.1">So many guests invite as here are writ.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit First Servant</i></p>
<a name="4.2.2">Sirrah, go hire me twenty cunning cooks.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>Second Servant</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.3">You shall have none ill, sir; for I'll try if they</a><br>
<a name="4.2.4">can lick their fingers.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.5">How canst thou try them so?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>Second Servant</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.6">Marry, sir, 'tis an ill cook that cannot lick his</a><br>
<a name="4.2.7">own fingers: therefore he that cannot lick his</a><br>
<a name="4.2.8">fingers goes not with me.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.9">Go, be gone.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit Second Servant</i></p>
<a name="4.2.10">We shall be much unfurnished for this time.</a><br>
<a name="4.2.11">What, is my daughter gone to Friar Laurence?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.12">Ay, forsooth.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.13">Well, he may chance to do some good on her:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.14">A peevish self-will'd harlotry it is.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.15">See where she comes from shrift with merry look.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter JULIET</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.16">How now, my headstrong! where have you been gadding?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.17">Where I have learn'd me to repent the sin</a><br>
<a name="4.2.18">Of disobedient opposition</a><br>
<a name="4.2.19">To you and your behests, and am enjoin'd</a><br>
<a name="4.2.20">By holy Laurence to fall prostrate here,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.21">And beg your pardon: pardon, I beseech you!</a><br>
<a name="4.2.22">Henceforward I am ever ruled by you.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.23">Send for the county; go tell him of this:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.24">I'll have this knot knit up to-morrow morning.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.25">I met the youthful lord at Laurence' cell;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.26">And gave him what becomed love I might,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.27">Not step o'er the bounds of modesty.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.28">Why, I am glad on't; this is well: stand up:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.29">This is as't should be. Let me see the county;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.30">Ay, marry, go, I say, and fetch him hither.</a><br>
<a name="4.2.31">Now, afore God! this reverend holy friar,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.32">Our whole city is much bound to him.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.33">Nurse, will you go with me into my closet,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.34">To help me sort such needful ornaments</a><br>
<a name="4.2.35">As you think fit to furnish me to-morrow?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.36">No, not till Thursday; there is time enough.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.37">Go, nurse, go with her: we'll to church to-morrow.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt JULIET and Nurse</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>LADY  CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.38">We shall be short in our provision:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.39">'Tis now near night.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.2.40">Tush, I will stir about,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.41">And all things shall be well, I warrant thee, wife:</a><br>
<a name="4.2.42">Go thou to Juliet, help to deck up her;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.43">I'll not to bed to-night; let me alone;</a><br>
<a name="4.2.44">I'll play the housewife for this once. What, ho!</a><br>
<a name="4.2.45">They are all forth. Well, I will walk myself</a><br>
<a name="4.2.46">To County Paris, to prepare him up</a><br>
<a name="4.2.47">Against to-morrow: my heart is wondrous light,</a><br>
<a name="4.2.48">Since this same wayward girl is so reclaim'd.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE III. Juliet's chamber.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter JULIET and Nurse</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.3.1">Ay, those attires are best: but, gentle nurse,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.2">I pray thee, leave me to my self to-night,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.3">For I have need of many orisons</a><br>
<a name="4.3.4">To move the heavens to smile upon my state,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.5">Which, well thou know'st, is cross, and full of sin.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter LADY CAPULET</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.3.6">What, are you busy, ho? need you my help?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.3.7">No, madam; we have cull'd such necessaries</a><br>
<a name="4.3.8">As are behoveful for our state to-morrow:</a><br>
<a name="4.3.9">So please you, let me now be left alone,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.10">And let the nurse this night sit up with you;</a><br>
<a name="4.3.11">For, I am sure, you have your hands full all,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.12">In this so sudden business.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.3.13">Good night:</a><br>
<a name="4.3.14">Get thee to bed, and rest; for thou hast need.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt LADY CAPULET and Nurse</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>JULIET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.3.15">Farewell! God knows when we shall meet again.</a><br>
<a name="4.3.16">I have a faint cold fear thrills through my veins,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.17">That almost freezes up the heat of life:</a><br>
<a name="4.3.18">I'll call them back again to comfort me:</a><br>
<a name="4.3.19">Nurse! What should she do here?</a><br>
<a name="4.3.20">My dismal scene I needs must act alone.</a><br>
<a name="4.3.21">Come, vial.</a><br>
<a name="4.3.22">What if this mixture do not work at all?</a><br>
<a name="4.3.23">Shall I be married then to-morrow morning?</a><br>
<a name="4.3.24">No, no: this shall forbid it: lie thou there.</a><br>
<p><i>Laying down her dagger</i></p>
<a name="4.3.25">What if it be a poison, which the friar</a><br>
<a name="4.3.26">Subtly hath minister'd to have me dead,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.27">Lest in this marriage he should be dishonour'd,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.28">Because he married me before to Romeo?</a><br>
<a name="4.3.29">I fear it is: and yet, methinks, it should not,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.30">For he hath still been tried a holy man.</a><br>
<a name="4.3.31">How if, when I am laid into the tomb,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.32">I wake before the time that Romeo</a><br>
<a name="4.3.33">Come to redeem me? there's a fearful point!</a><br>
<a name="4.3.34">Shall I not, then, be stifled in the vault,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.35">To whose foul mouth no healthsome air breathes in,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.36">And there die strangled ere my Romeo comes?</a><br>
<a name="4.3.37">Or, if I live, is it not very like,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.38">The horrible conceit of death and night,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.39">Together with the terror of the place,--</a><br>
<a name="4.3.40">As in a vault, an ancient receptacle,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.41">Where, for these many hundred years, the bones</a><br>
<a name="4.3.42">Of all my buried ancestors are packed:</a><br>
<a name="4.3.43">Where bloody Tybalt, yet but green in earth,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.44">Lies festering in his shroud; where, as they say,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.45">At some hours in the night spirits resort;--</a><br>
<a name="4.3.46">Alack, alack, is it not like that I,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.47">So early waking, what with loathsome smells,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.48">And shrieks like mandrakes' torn out of the earth,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.49">That living mortals, hearing them, run mad:--</a><br>
<a name="4.3.50">O, if I wake, shall I not be distraught,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.51">Environed with all these hideous fears?</a><br>
<a name="4.3.52">And madly play with my forefather's joints?</a><br>
<a name="4.3.53">And pluck the mangled Tybalt from his shroud?</a><br>
<a name="4.3.54">And, in this rage, with some great kinsman's bone,</a><br>
<a name="4.3.55">As with a club, dash out my desperate brains?</a><br>
<a name="4.3.56">O, look! methinks I see my cousin's ghost</a><br>
<a name="4.3.57">Seeking out Romeo, that did spit his body</a><br>
<a name="4.3.58">Upon a rapier's point: stay, Tybalt, stay!</a><br>
<a name="4.3.59">Romeo, I come! this do I drink to thee.</a><br>
<p><i>She falls upon her bed, within the curtains</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE IV. Hall in Capulet's house.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter LADY CAPULET and Nurse</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.4.1">Hold, take these keys, and fetch more spices, nurse.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.4.2">They call for dates and quinces in the pastry.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter CAPULET</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.4.3">Come, stir, stir, stir! the second cock hath crow'd,</a><br>
<a name="4.4.4">The curfew-bell hath rung, 'tis three o'clock:</a><br>
<a name="4.4.5">Look to the baked meats, good Angelica:</a><br>
<a name="4.4.6">Spare not for the cost.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.4.7">Go, you cot-quean, go,</a><br>
<a name="4.4.8">Get you to bed; faith, You'll be sick to-morrow</a><br>
<a name="4.4.9">For this night's watching.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.4.10">No, not a whit: what! I have watch'd ere now</a><br>
<a name="4.4.11">All night for lesser cause, and ne'er been sick.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.4.12">Ay, you have been a mouse-hunt in your time;</a><br>
<a name="4.4.13">But I will watch you from such watching now.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt LADY CAPULET and Nurse</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.4.14">A jealous hood, a jealous hood!</a><br>
<p><i>Enter three or four Servingmen, with spits, logs, and baskets</i></p>
<a name="4.4.15">Now, fellow,</a><br>
<a name="4.4.16">What's there?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>First Servant</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.4.17">Things for the cook, sir; but I know not what.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.4.18">Make haste, make haste.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit First Servant</i></p>
<a name="4.4.19">Sirrah, fetch drier logs:</a><br>
<a name="4.4.20">Call Peter, he will show thee where they are.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>Second Servant</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.4.21">I have a head, sir, that will find out logs,</a><br>
<a name="4.4.22">And never trouble Peter for the matter.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.4.23">Mass, and well said; a merry whoreson, ha!</a><br>
<a name="4.4.24">Thou shalt be logger-head. Good faith, 'tis day:</a><br>
<a name="4.4.25">The county will be here with music straight,</a><br>
<a name="4.4.26">For so he said he would: I hear him near.</a><br>
<p><i>Music within</i></p>
<a name="4.4.27">Nurse! Wife! What, ho! What, nurse, I say!</a><br>
<p><i>Re-enter Nurse</i></p>
<a name="4.4.28">Go waken Juliet, go and trim her up;</a><br>
<a name="4.4.29">I'll go and chat with Paris: hie, make haste,</a><br>
<a name="4.4.30">Make haste; the bridegroom he is come already:</a><br>
<a name="4.4.31">Make haste, I say.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE V. Juliet's chamber.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter Nurse</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.1">Mistress! what, mistress! Juliet! fast, I warrant her, she:</a><br>
<a name="4.5.2">Why, lamb! why, lady! fie, you slug-a-bed!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.3">Why, love, I say! madam! sweet-heart! why, bride!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.4">What, not a word? you take your pennyworths now;</a><br>
<a name="4.5.5">Sleep for a week; for the next night, I warrant,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.6">The County Paris hath set up his rest,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.7">That you shall rest but little. God forgive me,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.8">Marry, and amen, how sound is she asleep!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.9">I must needs wake her. Madam, madam, madam!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.10">Ay, let the county take you in your bed;</a><br>
<a name="4.5.11">He'll fright you up, i' faith. Will it not be?</a><br>
<p><i>Undraws the curtains</i></p>
<a name="4.5.12">What, dress'd! and in your clothes! and down again!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.13">I must needs wake you; Lady! lady! lady!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.14">Alas, alas! Help, help! my lady's dead!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.15">O, well-a-day, that ever I was born!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.16">Some aqua vitae, ho! My lord! my lady!</a><br>
<p><i>Enter LADY CAPULET</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.17">What noise is here?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.18">O lamentable day!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.19">What is the matter?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.20">Look, look! O heavy day!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.21">O me, O me! My child, my only life,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.22">Revive, look up, or I will die with thee!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.23">Help, help! Call help.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter CAPULET</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.24">For shame, bring Juliet forth; her lord is come.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.25">She's dead, deceased, she's dead; alack the day!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.26">Alack the day, she's dead, she's dead, she's dead!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.27">Ha! let me see her: out, alas! she's cold:</a><br>
<a name="4.5.28">Her blood is settled, and her joints are stiff;</a><br>
<a name="4.5.29">Life and these lips have long been separated:</a><br>
<a name="4.5.30">Death lies on her like an untimely frost</a><br>
<a name="4.5.31">Upon the sweetest flower of all the field.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.32">O lamentable day!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.33">                  O woful time!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.34">Death, that hath ta'en her hence to make me wail,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.35">Ties up my tongue, and will not let me speak.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter FRIAR LAURENCE and PARIS, with Musicians</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.36">Come, is the bride ready to go to church?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.37">Ready to go, but never to return.</a><br>
<a name="4.5.38">O son! the night before thy wedding-day</a><br>
<a name="4.5.39">Hath Death lain with thy wife. There she lies,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.40">Flower as she was, deflowered by him.</a><br>
<a name="4.5.41">Death is my son-in-law, Death is my heir;</a><br>
<a name="4.5.42">My daughter he hath wedded: I will die,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.43">And leave him all; life, living, all is Death's.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech16"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.44">Have I thought long to see this morning's face,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.45">And doth it give me such a sight as this?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech17"><b>LADY CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.46">Accursed, unhappy, wretched, hateful day!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.47">Most miserable hour that e'er time saw</a><br>
<a name="4.5.48">In lasting labour of his pilgrimage!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.49">But one, poor one, one poor and loving child,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.50">But one thing to rejoice and solace in,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.51">And cruel death hath catch'd it from my sight!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech18"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.52">O woe! O woful, woful, woful day!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.53">Most lamentable day, most woful day,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.54">That ever, ever, I did yet behold!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.55">O day! O day! O day! O hateful day!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.56">Never was seen so black a day as this:</a><br>
<a name="4.5.57">O woful day, O woful day!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech19"><b>PARIS</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.58">Beguiled, divorced, wronged, spited, slain!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.59">Most detestable death, by thee beguil'd,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.60">By cruel cruel thee quite overthrown!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.61">O love! O life! not life, but love in death!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech20"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.62">Despised, distressed, hated, martyr'd, kill'd!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.63">Uncomfortable time, why camest thou now</a><br>
<a name="4.5.64">To murder, murder our solemnity?</a><br>
<a name="4.5.65">O child! O child! my soul, and not my child!</a><br>
<a name="4.5.66">Dead art thou! Alack! my child is dead;</a><br>
<a name="4.5.67">And with my child my joys are buried.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech21"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.68">Peace, ho, for shame! confusion's cure lives not</a><br>
<a name="4.5.69">In these confusions. Heaven and yourself</a><br>
<a name="4.5.70">Had part in this fair maid; now heaven hath all,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.71">And all the better is it for the maid:</a><br>
<a name="4.5.72">Your part in her you could not keep from death,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.73">But heaven keeps his part in eternal life.</a><br>
<a name="4.5.74">The most you sought was her promotion;</a><br>
<a name="4.5.75">For 'twas your heaven she should be advanced:</a><br>
<a name="4.5.76">And weep ye now, seeing she is advanced</a><br>
<a name="4.5.77">Above the clouds, as high as heaven itself?</a><br>
<a name="4.5.78">O, in this love, you love your child so ill,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.79">That you run mad, seeing that she is well:</a><br>
<a name="4.5.80">She's not well married that lives married long;</a><br>
<a name="4.5.81">But she's best married that dies married young.</a><br>
<a name="4.5.82">Dry up your tears, and stick your rosemary</a><br>
<a name="4.5.83">On this fair corse; and, as the custom is,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.84">In all her best array bear her to church:</a><br>
<a name="4.5.85">For though fond nature bids us an lament,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.86">Yet nature's tears are reason's merriment.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech22"><b>CAPULET</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.87">All things that we ordained festival,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.88">Turn from their office to black funeral;</a><br>
<a name="4.5.89">Our instruments to melancholy bells,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.90">Our wedding cheer to a sad burial feast,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.91">Our solemn hymns to sullen dirges change,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.92">Our bridal flowers serve for a buried corse,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.93">And all things change them to the contrary.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech23"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.94">Sir, go you in; and, madam, go with him;</a><br>
<a name="4.5.95">And go, Sir Paris; every one prepare</a><br>
<a name="4.5.96">To follow this fair corse unto her grave:</a><br>
<a name="4.5.97">The heavens do lour upon you for some ill;</a><br>
<a name="4.5.98">Move them no more by crossing their high will.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt CAPULET, LADY CAPULET, PARIS, and FRIAR LAURENCE</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech24"><b>First Musician</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.99">Faith, we may put up our pipes, and be gone.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech25"><b>Nurse</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.100">Honest goodfellows, ah, put up, put up;</a><br>
<a name="4.5.101">For, well you know, this is a pitiful case.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech26"><b>First Musician</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.102">Ay, by my troth, the case may be amended.</a><br>
<p><i>Enter PETER</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech27"><b>PETER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.103">Musicians, O, musicians, 'Heart's ease, Heart's</a><br>
<a name="4.5.104">ease:' O, an you will have me live, play 'Heart's ease.'</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech28"><b>First Musician</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.105">Why 'Heart's ease?'</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech29"><b>PETER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.106">O, musicians, because my heart itself plays 'My</a><br>
<a name="4.5.107">heart is full of woe:' O, play me some merry dump,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.108">to comfort me.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech30"><b>First Musician</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.109">Not a dump we; 'tis no time to play now.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech31"><b>PETER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.110">You will not, then?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech32"><b>First Musician</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.111">No.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech33"><b>PETER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.112">I will then give it you soundly.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech34"><b>First Musician</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.113">What will you give us?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech35"><b>PETER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.114">No money, on my faith, but the gleek;</a><br>
<a name="4.5.115">I will give you the minstrel.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech36"><b>First Musician</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.116">Then I will give you the serving-creature.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech37"><b>PETER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.117">Then will I lay the serving-creature's dagger on</a><br>
<a name="4.5.118">your pate. I will carry no crotchets: I'll re you,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.119">I'll fa you; do you note me?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech38"><b>First Musician</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.120">An you re us and fa us, you note us.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech39"><b>Second Musician</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.121">Pray you, put up your dagger, and put out your wit.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech40"><b>PETER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.122">Then have at you with my wit! I will dry-beat you</a><br>
<a name="4.5.123">with an iron wit, and put up my iron dagger. Answer</a><br>
<a name="4.5.124">me like men:</a><br>
<a name="4.5.125">'When griping grief the heart doth wound,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.126">And doleful dumps the mind oppress,</a><br>
<a name="4.5.127">Then music with her silver sound'--</a><br>
<a name="4.5.128">why 'silver sound'? why 'music with her silver</a><br>
<a name="4.5.129">sound'? What say you, Simon Catling?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech41"><b>Musician</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.130">Marry, sir, because silver hath a sweet sound.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech42"><b>PETER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.131">Pretty! What say you, Hugh Rebeck?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech43"><b>Second Musician</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.132">I say 'silver sound,' because musicians sound for silver.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech44"><b>PETER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.133">Pretty too! What say you, James Soundpost?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech45"><b>Third Musician</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.134">Faith, I know not what to say.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech46"><b>PETER</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.135">O, I cry you mercy; you are the singer: I will say</a><br>
<a name="4.5.136">for you. It is 'music with her silver sound,'</a><br>
<a name="4.5.137">because musicians have no gold for sounding:</a><br>
<a name="4.5.138">'Then music with her silver sound</a><br>
<a name="4.5.139">With speedy help doth lend redress.'</a><br>
<p><i>Exit</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech47"><b>First Musician</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.140">What a pestilent knave is this same!</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech48"><b>Second Musician</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="4.5.141">Hang him, Jack! Come, we'll in here; tarry for the</a><br>
<a name="4.5.142">mourners, and stay dinner.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote><p>
</p><h3>ACT V</h3>
<h3>SCENE I. Mantua. A street.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter ROMEO</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.1">If I may trust the flattering truth of sleep,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.2">My dreams presage some joyful news at hand:</a><br>
<a name="5.1.3">My bosom's lord sits lightly in his throne;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.4">And all this day an unaccustom'd spirit</a><br>
<a name="5.1.5">Lifts me above the ground with cheerful thoughts.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.6">I dreamt my lady came and found me dead--</a><br>
<a name="5.1.7">Strange dream, that gives a dead man leave</a><br>
<a name="5.1.8">to think!--</a><br>
<a name="5.1.9">And breathed such life with kisses in my lips,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.10">That I revived, and was an emperor.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.11">Ah me! how sweet is love itself possess'd,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.12">When but love's shadows are so rich in joy!</a><br>
<p><i>Enter BALTHASAR, booted</i></p>
<a name="5.1.13">News from Verona!--How now, Balthasar!</a><br>
<a name="5.1.14">Dost thou not bring me letters from the friar?</a><br>
<a name="5.1.15">How doth my lady? Is my father well?</a><br>
<a name="5.1.16">How fares my Juliet? that I ask again;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.17">For nothing can be ill, if she be well.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>BALTHASAR</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.18">Then she is well, and nothing can be ill:</a><br>
<a name="5.1.19">Her body sleeps in Capel's monument,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.20">And her immortal part with angels lives.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.21">I saw her laid low in her kindred's vault,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.22">And presently took post to tell it you:</a><br>
<a name="5.1.23">O, pardon me for bringing these ill news,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.24">Since you did leave it for my office, sir.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.25">Is it even so? then I defy you, stars!</a><br>
<a name="5.1.26">Thou know'st my lodging: get me ink and paper,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.27">And hire post-horses; I will hence to-night.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech4"><b>BALTHASAR</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.28">I do beseech you, sir, have patience:</a><br>
<a name="5.1.29">Your looks are pale and wild, and do import</a><br>
<a name="5.1.30">Some misadventure.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech5"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.31">                  Tush, thou art deceived:</a><br>
<a name="5.1.32">Leave me, and do the thing I bid thee do.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.33">Hast thou no letters to me from the friar?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech6"><b>BALTHASAR</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.34">No, my good lord.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech7"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.35">                  No matter: get thee gone,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.36">And hire those horses; I'll be with thee straight.</a><br>
<p><i>Exit BALTHASAR</i></p>
<a name="5.1.37">Well, Juliet, I will lie with thee to-night.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.38">Let's see for means: O mischief, thou art swift</a><br>
<a name="5.1.39">To enter in the thoughts of desperate men!</a><br>
<a name="5.1.40">I do remember an apothecary,--</a><br>
<a name="5.1.41">And hereabouts he dwells,--which late I noted</a><br>
<a name="5.1.42">In tatter'd weeds, with overwhelming brows,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.43">Culling of simples; meagre were his looks,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.44">Sharp misery had worn him to the bones:</a><br>
<a name="5.1.45">And in his needy shop a tortoise hung,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.46">An alligator stuff'd, and other skins</a><br>
<a name="5.1.47">Of ill-shaped fishes; and about his shelves</a><br>
<a name="5.1.48">A beggarly account of empty boxes,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.49">Green earthen pots, bladders and musty seeds,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.50">Remnants of packthread and old cakes of roses,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.51">Were thinly scatter'd, to make up a show.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.52">Noting this penury, to myself I said</a><br>
<a name="5.1.53">'An if a man did need a poison now,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.54">Whose sale is present death in Mantua,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.55">Here lives a caitiff wretch would sell it him.'</a><br>
<a name="5.1.56">O, this same thought did but forerun my need;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.57">And this same needy man must sell it me.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.58">As I remember, this should be the house.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.59">Being holiday, the beggar's shop is shut.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.60">What, ho! apothecary!</a><br>
<p><i>Enter Apothecary</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech8"><b>Apothecary</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.61">Who calls so loud?</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech9"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.62">Come hither, man. I see that thou art poor:</a><br>
<a name="5.1.63">Hold, there is forty ducats: let me have</a><br>
<a name="5.1.64">A dram of poison, such soon-speeding gear</a><br>
<a name="5.1.65">As will disperse itself through all the veins</a><br>
<a name="5.1.66">That the life-weary taker may fall dead</a><br>
<a name="5.1.67">And that the trunk may be discharged of breath</a><br>
<a name="5.1.68">As violently as hasty powder fired</a><br>
<a name="5.1.69">Doth hurry from the fatal cannon's womb.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech10"><b>Apothecary</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.70">Such mortal drugs I have; but Mantua's law</a><br>
<a name="5.1.71">Is death to any he that utters them.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech11"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.72">Art thou so bare and full of wretchedness,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.73">And fear'st to die? famine is in thy cheeks,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.74">Need and oppression starveth in thine eyes,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.75">Contempt and beggary hangs upon thy back;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.76">The world is not thy friend nor the world's law;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.77">The world affords no law to make thee rich;</a><br>
<a name="5.1.78">Then be not poor, but break it, and take this.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech12"><b>Apothecary</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.79">My poverty, but not my will, consents.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech13"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.80">I pay thy poverty, and not thy will.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech14"><b>Apothecary</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.81">Put this in any liquid thing you will,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.82">And drink it off; and, if you had the strength</a><br>
<a name="5.1.83">Of twenty men, it would dispatch you straight.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech15"><b>ROMEO</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.1.84">There is thy gold, worse poison to men's souls,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.85">Doing more murders in this loathsome world,</a><br>
<a name="5.1.86">Than these poor compounds that thou mayst not sell.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.87">I sell thee poison; thou hast sold me none.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.88">Farewell: buy food, and get thyself in flesh.</a><br>
<a name="5.1.89">Come, cordial and not poison, go with me</a><br>
<a name="5.1.90">To Juliet's grave; for there must I use thee.</a><br>
<p><i>Exeunt</i></p>
</blockquote>
<h3>SCENE II. Friar Laurence's cell.</h3>
<p></p><blockquote>
<i>Enter FRIAR JOHN</i>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech1"><b>FRIAR JOHN</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.1">Holy Franciscan friar! brother, ho!</a><br>
<p><i>Enter FRIAR LAURENCE</i></p>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech2"><b>FRIAR LAURENCE</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.2">This same should be the voice of Friar John.</a><br>
<a name="5.2.3">Welcome from Mantua: what says Romeo?</a><br>
<a name="5.2.4">Or, if his mind be writ, give me his letter.</a><br>
</blockquote>

<a name="speech3"><b>FRIAR JOHN</b></a>
<blockquote>
<a name="5.2.5">Going to find a bare-foot brother out</a><br>
<a name="5.2.6">One of our order, to associate me,</a><br>
